<< 2 Corinthians 6:18

> Which of you, if his son asks for bread, will give him a stone? Or if he asks for a fish, will give him a snake? So if you who are evil know how to give good gifts to your children, how much more will your Father in heaven give good things to those who ask Him!

<< Matthew 7:9–11

> I am the *everlasting* Father, and I will be a Father to you; I take you for my sons and daughters. Behold, I will receive you not as servants, but as sons, to abide in my house for ever; whatever love or care children may look for from *their father*, that may you expect from me; and *so much more as I am wiser, and greater and better* than any earthly parents. If earthly fathers will give good things to their children, much more will I give to you. If such *cannot forget* their children, much less will I forget you. What *would my children have*? Your Father's *heart*, and your Father's *house*; your Father's *care*, and your Father's *ear*; Your Father's *bread*, and your Father's *rod*, these shall be all yours.

<< Joseph Alleine, *The Voice of God*

> If parents do this by rightly training them to God's service, they will indeed have their hands full of good works. For what are the hungry, the thirsty, the naked, the sick, the alien if not the souls of your own children? With these God makes a hospital of your own house. He sets you over them as the hospital superintendent, to wait on them, to give them the food and drink of good words and works. [He sets you over them] that they may learn to trust God, to believe in him, to fear him, and to set their whole hope upon him; to honor his name and never curse or swear; to mortify themselves by praying, fasting, watching, working; to go to church, wait on the word of God, and observe the sabbath. [He sets you over them] that they may learn to despise temporal things, to bear misfortune without complaint, and neither fear death nor love this life. Just think what a great lesson this is! How many good works you have at hand in your own home with your own child who needs all such things as these like a hungry, thirsty, naked, poor, imprisoned, sick soul! O what a blessed marriage and home that would be with such parents! That home would indeed be a true church, a chosen cloister, yes, a paradise!

<< Martin Luther, *Treatise on Good Works*

## Series' Preface

#### Passage: Crossing Thresholds in the Sojourn of Life

##### Series' Preface

> These are the journeys of the Israelites when they came out of the land of Egypt by their divisions under the leadership of Moses and Aaron. At the Lord's command, Moses recorded the stages of their journey. These are the stages listed by their starting points.

<< Numbers 33:1–2

> Moses wrote their goings out—When they set out, God ordered him to keep a journal of all the remarkable occurrences in the way, that it might be a satisfaction to himself and an instruction to Others. It may be of use to Christians, to preserve an account of the providences of God concerning them, the constant series of mercies they have experienced, and especially those turns which have made some days of their lives more remarkable.

<< *John Wesley, Explanatory Notes upon the Old Testament*

Growth in life and faith occurs in stages of development. Just as there are common markers for biological maturity, so can we discern noteworthy milestones in spiritual formation. In order to help individuals and communities mature, we do well to mark and celebrate these milestones. Even better, to foster intentional spiritual formation that coincides with natural life development is a compelling vision to help establish believers and churches in the faith. Such a process would offer a helpful means of passing down "the faith entrusted once for all to the saints" (Jude 3). Certainly, maturity in the faith has an intergenerational scope: delivering the ancient faith intact and unalloyed to the future generation. As such, processes and strategies of Christian formation must pay special attention to natural phases of human development and commonly discerned dynamics of spiritual growth.

With this vision of Christian spirituality, one can imagine a number of ways to foster Christian maturity across the generations. In fact, faithful disciples and church leaders have diligently discharged this duty through the ages. We must learn from them as humble apprentices while we also consider the unique context of our contemporary world. Christians have a duty to both preservation and innovation. On the one hand, we must heed Paul's instructions to Timothy: "guard the treasure entrusted to you, with the help of the Holy Spirit who dwells in us" (2 Timothy 1:14). On the other hand, we must be like the men of Issachar "who understood the times and knew what Israel should do" (1 Chronicles 12:32). The Christian life is lived at the intersection of timeless truth and contemporary dilemma, where God's inspired word addresses the great need of mankind. Until the return of Christ, a time we long for with great anticipation, we will constantly live at this crossroads. And, the complexity of the crossroads for each age serves as a fortuitous opportunity—an ever-ripe ministry occasion—to prove the relevance and veracity of God's timeless truth in all cultures, for all generations, and in all developmental stages.

To this end, we offer this series as a tool in Christian formation. Essentially, it is a *Christian approach to rites of passage*. The title of the series speaks of its fundamental aims: *Passage: Crossing Thresholds in the Sojourn of Life*. At its core, this tool views the Christian life as a sojourn. More than a temporary excursion or one-time outing, the voyage of life is an extended pilgrimage that encompasses our whole life. This sojourn is also a journey of advancement, development, and maturation across successive stages. The overall forward movement of the Christian life—even if it is necessarily fraught with its detours, roundabouts, diversions, and wanderings—occurs across various phases of progress. Because there are phases of development in life and faith, we are (at times) able to mark the thresholds when we cross from one phase into the next, from one region into another.

To become aware of your current location in the journey and to be cognizant of upcoming legs of the pilgrimage is quite helpful. It can provide encouragement for the weary, humility for the proud, motivation for the slothful, and hope for all. Crossing thresholds together with other travelers—a company of trusted fellow pilgrims—provides camaraderie, companionship, and solidarity for the difficult terrain that we must traverse. As we cross these thresholds together, we are making passage into new territory. These passages will be myriad in life. To mark the successful arrival at each juncture in a journey affords a time of reflection on the past, celebration in the present, and preparation for the future. Marking these milestones of life and faith helps pilgrims remain oriented to the road ahead, focused on the final destination, and vigilant against many dangers and snares they might face. Said shortly, this series aims to help Christian disciples mark, measure, and make passage through important milestones for life and faith.

Many cultures and societies have rites of passage. Typically, these ritualbased experiences assist a person (and the community) in explicitly marking a change of status in life and development. Broadly speaking, these rites of passage can mark many seasons of life: birth, puberty (coming of age), marriage, childbearing, graduation, vocational achievement, eldership, and death. Commonly, the rite itself involves a temporary separation from society, an initiation through a challenge, and a reintegration with the community under a new status.

It must be said that Christians are not beholden to cultural rites, rituals, and ceremonies. In Christ, believers have been set free from the man-made tradition (Matthew 15:1–20) and man-made religion (Colossians 2:20–23). Nevertheless, to ritualize an experience is nothing other than attempting to invite community members to participate in an experience with regularity, repetition, and patterned form. Participation in such an experience helps to shape us. For the church, Christ has only commanded two ritual practices: baptism and the Lord's Supper (communion or the eucharist). Baptism is a one-time initiation rite into the community of faith. The Lord's Supper is an ongoing revitalizing ritual to sustain and support God's people through an act of covenant renewal. Even though other rituals are not commanded in Scripture, we offer this Christian approach to rites of passage as a way of marking major milestones of life and faith. These are not ever to be held with the same status as baptism and the Lord's Supper. Nevertheless, they offer a concrete way to guide people and communities in faith formation.

By labeling this series *Passage*, this curricular approach to rites of passage focuses on the borderlands: *the liminal moments and crucial junctures when we cross over into a new phase of our faith journey*. Different than an establishing process or catechetical course, this series aims to measure, mark, and commemorate transitions across seasons of life and faith. Sometimes people cross into new life seasons without realizing it. Other times they venture across a major life threshold without guide or escort. When we remain unaware that a major life milestone has been reach or unguided across its threshold, we can unnecessarily prolong immature habits and life patterns from an earlier phase of life. On the contrary, to prematurely encumber a person with responsibilities before they are ready can be overwhelming (at least) and detrimental (at worst). Consequently, this series has a *preparatory* and *demarcating* function. It seeks to help a person understand to consider what an upcoming season of life might entail while it also helps to make this seasonal transition explicit and noticeable.

Over the course of this series, we hope to provide concrete and flexible tools for navigating the transitions in life and faith. This curriculum is unapologetically Christian. While people may be able to use the tools to discern general principles of life formation, this series is explicitly a summons to faithful Christian pilgrimage across life's thresholds. As such, the composition of this tool is designed to be church-based, family-oriented, biblically-rooted, and theologically-reflective. In the end, we humbly offer this as a gift to the church so that believers and communities might join the psalmist's words as a refrain of life: God's "statutes are songs to me in the house of my pilgrimage" (Psalm 119:54).

<< July 2024
<< York, Pennsylvania

## Preface

#### HomeStead: Building Christian Hearth and Home

##### Preface

> Then our sons will be like plants nurtured in their youth, our daughters like corner pillars carved to adorn a palace.

<< Psalm 144:12

> The power for good or evil that resides in a little child is great beyond all human calculation. A child rightly trained may be a world-wide blessing, with an influence reaching onward to eternal years. But a neglected or misdirected child may live to blight and blast mankind, and leave influences of evil which shall roll on in increasing volume till they plunge into the gulf of eternal perdition.

<< George Müller, *A Brief Narrative*

Cultivating Christian hearth and home is honorable and holy work. Parents, who are the primary overseers of this work, must view their parenting responsibilities as a sacred and solemn duty, consecrating their children and household to the Lord. Two main scriptural examples explicitly narrative this act of dedication and consecration. In the Old Testament, when Hannah devoted young Samuel to the Lord, she was acknowledging that her child was a gracious gift from God (cf. 1 Samuel 1:1–2:26). She modeled for us the importance of raising children in the faith and for God's service. In the New Testament, when Jesus' parents dedicated the infant Christ to God, they were also surrendering their home to the Lord (Luke 2:21–40). Their example of dedication is a compelling example for us to cultivate a home devoted to God.

In this study, we want to consider what it means for us to devote our homes to the Lord, bringing our marriages, families, houses, and children as an offering to God. Some of us may have children already and others may be looking forward to the start of the parenting journey. Some are grandparents who are envisioning this last season of life as an investment into future generations, which means pouring into younger parents in their efforts to establish a godly home. Other are part of the general community of faith and realize their own duty to help families become strong and healthy. Young or old, these faithful saints engage this study to support families, knowing that parenting is intrinsically a community venture. You are here to help God's people raise children to be devoted saints. Wherever you are in this journey, we invite you to consider what it means to cross a major life milestone and threshold into the world of parenting—to build Christian hearth and home for the Lord.

To help build a vision for biblical parenting, we have chosen a selection of psalms to guide our thinking about parenting. Really, the collection of these psalms offers *a theological vision for the life of faith*. The underlying principles in these psalms are general enough that they form a solid foundation for all ages to embrace a life devoted to the Lord. Precisely because they are common core elements to the life of faith they are fitting for a discussion on biblical parenting. As we will see, biblical parenting is primarily a matter of passing on the faith to the next generation. In these selected psalms, the enduring principles about the life of faith help to establish parents in their role before God, their duty toward their children, a work alongside the church, and a mission for the sake of the world. As we will see, a vision for life with God in the context of God's people and on God's mission provides a beautiful landscape upon which we can portray godly parenting.

Assuredly, this selection of psalms is not haphazard. Even if in small detail, each of these psalms makes an explicit signal toward family life or parenting in some way. Psalm 103 depicts God as a compassionate father (103:13). Psalm 78 explicitly casts a vision of passing the faith to children and future generations (78:1–8). Psalm 23 describes God as a good shepherd who prepares a table in the midst of enemies (23:5). Psalm 34 directly summons children to learn the fear of God (34:11). Psalm 94 soberly describes the importance of discipline and instruction (94:10–12). Psalm 127 and 128 celebrate housebuilding, the blessing of children, and flourishing homes by painting an inspiring picture of a godly home that thrives under the Lord's favor (127:1, 3–5; 128:3–4, 6).

The basic premise of this study is that *we should parent our children based upon God's divine care for us*. First, this means that God is *the ultimate example of parenting*. His love, care, faithfulness, discipline, instruction, protection, and house order should serve as a blueprint for how we raise and rear our children. But God is more than an example to his people. A more important second point is that God is *the source of all Christian parenting*. His compassion toward us is fuel that enables us to show sacrificial love and unconditional care toward our children. His unchanging truth is the only sure foundation of our instruction—a deposit that can fortify generations. His tender shepherding care supports us during our care of our children, as we recognize how weak and needy we really are. God's discipline and instruction in our own lives better equips us to teach our children the fear of God and model godly character. God's protection is the only means by which our children can find safety and security in a godless and wicked world. His blessing, favor, enablement, and strength are the only hope for building a home that honors the Lord.

While we could have chosen many other psalms to serve as a biblical bedrock for godly parenting, we chose the following ones because of they present a fairly complete picture of major areas of faith formation, family life, and parenting duty. Psalm 103:1–22 highlights the importance of worship, the essence of divine compassion, God's gracious care of his people, and the foundation of covenant relationship with God. This psalm calls God's people to find rest under God's fatherly care. Godly parents rely upon God's divine compassion and model it in their homes. Psalm 78:1–72 casts a vision for teaching truth—the story of God's words and works—in an intergenerational and community context that leads people into devotion of God. This psalm shows that each generation stands between the ages in our life of faith and ministry work. Godly parents bear the responsibility of passing the faith to the next generation, with a vision of impacting many generations to come. Psalm 23:1–6 celebrates God as our shepherd, finding comfort in his provision, guidance, protection, correction, presence, and blessing. This psalm asserts God's sovereign and tender care over his flock. Godly parents should approach their parenting work as under-shepherds who are being shepherded by God care.

Psalm 34:1–22 commends to us a life of continual worship in the fear of God, in which we grow in godly character and can embrace the vocation of a righteous sufferer. This psalm calls us to live life in the fear of God. Godly parents focus their parenting on faith formation and character development. Psalm 94:1–23 focuses on the godlessness of surrounding society, the need of divine discipline and instruction, and the comfort of divine protection. This psalm invites us to hide in God's sheltering protection. Godly parents raise their children in the discipline and instruction of the Lord. Psalm 127:1–5 and 128:1–6 offer a portrait of a godly family, emphasizing themes of meaningful work, genuine faith, divine blessing, and the central importance of family in God's work in the world. This psalm invites us to be for the world by being for the family. Godly parents treasure children as gifts from God and depend upon God's grace to build their house.

What we will find in these psalms is not practical how-tos or parenting techniques for raising children. The collective of hymns offers something much bigger and much more important. These psalms offer *a theological vision for the Christian life*. Because these principles guide us in a life devoted to God, they are inextricably bound to parenting. Whatever else parenting and family life entail, they are at their core an effort to build a small-scale, home-based community that mirrors the the larger community of faith joining together in worship and devotion to God. These psalms are about *parenting* precisely because they are about *cultivating our life with God*.

Although technique and tactic are helpful and have their place, they will not ultimately sustain you in the parenting work. Children are unique, circumstances change, cultural challenges multiply, daily needs fluctuate, and seasons of life demand different strategies and interventions. At the beginning of the parenting journey (and even as reminders throughout), what is needed is an unchanging vision for parenting that encapsulates the essence of our life with God. Such a theological vision for parenting can sustain you in suffering, comfort you in disappointment, focus you when confronted with cultural confusion, challenge you in your own personal formation, form tenacity in the face of opposition, and build stamina for the long haul of parenting work. It can also help you realize the deep joy of parenting work. A vision for life with God is the best starting point for entering into this most noble of ventures.

The godly raising of children is to participate in God's work in the world. A theological vision for parenting is crucial because however long and unending parenting work feels (and the days do feel long), it will too soon be over (the years do go fast), and the foundations we laid will set our children on a path toward some eternal destiny. May we parent out of a life with God that enables us to establish hearth and home after God's designed and fueled by God's gracious supply and one that lays up our children as "treasures in heaven" (Matthew 6:20).

<< March 2026
<< York, Pennsylvania

# PART ONE: Preparation

In **Part One** of this rite of passage, you will study selected psalms that give you a *theological vision for parenting*. Specifically, you will study these psalms to help you understand the core principles to a life devoted to God. These foundations will give you a solid base from which to understand the noble duty of parenting. The foundation of this study will be based upon six sessions that engage biblical teaching, cultivate community devotion, encourage personal reflection, and facilitate spiritual disciplines. As you explore these core sessions, you will discover six crucial areas of building Christian hearth and home: *worship*, *intergenerationality*, *shepherding care*, *character development*, *discipline*, and *mission*. These sessions will explore various parental duties: cultivating devotion to our heavenly Father, passing the faith tradition across generations, caring as shepherds of a family flock, moral formation in the fear of God, parental protection in godly discipline, and raising children for world impact. The introductory session helps you (and those in your support community) get oriented to this process. The concluding session provides a time for reflective review as well as preparing you for the next major part of the rite of passage.

## Introduction

#### No Longer Two: Receiving Children as Gifts from God

##### Overview

> "I prayed for this boy, and since the Lord has granted me what I asked of Him, I now dedicate the boy to the Lord. For as long as he lives, he is dedicated to the Lord." So they worshiped the Lord there.

<< 1 Samuel 1:27–28

> Parents, consider the condition of your children; and the loud cry of their condition unto you, to endeavour their salvation! .... Know you not, that your children have precious and immortal souls within them? The are not all flesh. You That are the parents of their flesh, must know, that your children have spirits also ... Are you solicitous that their bodies may be fed? You should be more solicitous that their souls may not be starved, or go without the Bread of Life. Are you solicitous that their bodies may be cloath'd: you should be more solicitous, that their souls may not be naked, or go without the garments of righteousness.

<< Cotton Mather, *A Family Well-Ordered*

This opening session serves as an orientation for a rite of passage into Christian homebuilding. Specifically, this rite of passage celebrates and marks the journey of raising children and building a home in a way that honors and glorifies God. No longer two (just husband and wife), you are welcoming children into your family. The stakes are high. The investment made in a child's life in its early years can profoundly shape them. But the joys are greater than the challenges. Few things are more rewarding than to see children grow in godliness, embrace the truth, take ownership of their faith, worship the Lord, and serve God's purposes in the world. In this introductory session, you will preview the overall structure of this process and explore the underlying rationale for such a rite of passage. Additionally, you will study a core passage from the life of Jesus that highlights this milestone in his earthly life. Parents, spiritual mentors, trusted peers, church leaders, and the wider church community are all integral to this journey into parenting. These key people are invited to this introductory session so that they can support and encourage you as you gain a theological vision for parenting your family in life with God. The starting point to this great and noble work is the realization that our families belong first and foremost to the Lord.

##### Key Idea

Our home belongs to the Lord.

### Introduction Community Study

#### Tuning Our Hearts to the Lord

*Unite your hearts together in worship. Use the community confession, corporate song, Scripture reading, responsive call, and opening prayer to enter God's presence together.*

##### Community Confession

**By God's grace and through faith in Christ, our home belongs to the Lord.** As priests, we will nurture our children in God's fatherly care. As stewards, we will pass on the faith of our fathers. As shepherds, we will lead our children as God shepherds us. As teachers, we will instruct our household in the fear of the Lord. As protectors, we will entrust our family to the God's safekeeping. As overseers, we will build Christian hearth and home as a heritage from the Lord. As for me and my house, we will serve the Lord. For God's glory, for our good, and for the sake of the world! Amen and amen!

##### Corporate Song

###### Be Thou My Vision

**Verse 1**
Be thou my vision,  
O Lord of my heart;  
Naught be all else to me  
Save that thou art.  
Thou my best thought  
By day and by night;  
Waking or sleeping, thy presence  
My light.

**Verse 2**
Be thou my wisdom,  
And thou my true Word;  
I ever with thee  
And thou with me, Lord.  
Thou my great Father,  
I thy dear child;  
Thou in me dwelling,  
With thee reconciled.

**Verse 3**
Riches I heed not,  
Nor vain, empty praise;  
Thou mine inheritance,  
Now and always.  
Thou and thou only,  
First in my heart,  
High King of Heaven,  
My treasure thou art.

**Verse 4**
High King of Heaven,  
My victory won,  
May I reach Heaven’s joys,  
O bright Heav’ns Sun!  
Heart of my heart,  
Whatever befall,  
Still be my vision,  
O Ruler of all

##### Scripture Reading

But the chief priests and scribes were indignant when they saw the wonders He performed and the children shouting in the temple courts, "Hosanna to the Son of David!" "Do You hear what these children are saying?" they asked. "Yes," Jesus answered. "Have you never read: 'From the mouths of children and infants You have ordained praise'?"

<< Matthew 21:15–16

##### Responsive Call

**Reader**: Hallelujah! Sing to the Lord a new song—His praise in the assembly of the godly.

**All**: Let Israel rejoice in their Maker; let the children of Zion rejoice in their King.

**Reader**: Let them praise His name with dancing, and make music to Him with tambourine and harp.

**All**: For the Lord takes pleasure in His people; He adorns the afflicted with salvation.

**Reader**: Let the saints exult in glory; let them shout for joy upon their beds.

<< Psalm 149:1–5

##### Opening Prayer

Gracious Lord, we thank you for the gift of family. Inasmuch as marriage is a sacred covenant, parenting is a holy vocation. We want to raise our children before the Lord. So we come to consecrate our home, our family, our house, and our children to you. Help us build a home on the foundations of your word. Assist us in making our hearth and home hospitable. May our family order be a testimony to others. May our failures be opportunities to cling to your grace. May our hopes be rooted in your promises. May our ambitions be aligned to your will. Help us to kindle love in our home such that others may come to know the wonderful salvation that is in Christ. Help us, O God, we pray! Amen!

#### Seeking God's Wisdom

*Focus your thinking around the key idea and biblical wisdom. Review the introduction and discuss the scriptural proverb to help orient you to the main theme of this session.*

##### Introduction

> But if it is unpleasing in your sight to serve the Lord, then choose for yourselves this day whom you will serve, whether the gods your fathers served beyond the Euphrates, or the gods of the Amorites in whose land you are living. As for me and my house, we will serve the Lord!

<< Joshua 24:15

> Every Christian family ought to be, as it were, a little church consecrated to Christ, and wholly influenced and governed by His rules.

<< Jonathan Edwards, *Sermon*

You are no longer just two. At this season of life, you are welcoming children into your family. This is God's design. It's is a beautiful blessing and a weighty responsibility. You likely feel both of these competing realities simultaneously. At times, you are stricken by the gift of your child (or children), grateful for the life of this child intricately fashioned in the image of God. Other times you are overwhelmed by the new duties and countless activities involved in nurturing your little ones. As God allows, the blessing of marriage eventual turns into the blessing of parenting. According to God's design, a husband and wife committed to each other in the covenant of marriage are called to be fruitful, bear children, and establish a family. Sometimes a family is established through adoption and fostering of other children. In all cases, healthy families are foundational to society. Healthy families throughout the world are best grown when they have a vision for being a godly family before the Lord. Rather than merely wandering into this parenting role, this study will seek to mark this milestone in your life in a way that helps you think about your parenting vocation from a biblical and God-honoring perspective. Godly parenting begins with consecrating our family and children to the Lord. In fact, it begins with fully dedicating ourselves, as parents, to God. In this session, we will overview the process of this rite of passage and discuss a biblical foundation for what we will experience in the weeks to come.

##### Biblical Wisdom

The righteous man walks with integrity; blessed are his children after him.

<< Proverbs 20:7

###### Core Principle

This biblical proverb gives us insight into the nature of Christian parenting. It teaches us that one of the main means of parenting is a parent's faith maturity and Christian character. The person who "walks with integrity" is considered "righteous." Essentially, a life fully consecrated to God results in a transformation of one's character. Those who live a life devoted to God can hope for God's favor on their children: "blessed are his children after him." This rite of passage process offers a pathway for you to mark your entrance into parenting: to order your home after God's design, to establish your home on Christ, to cultivate a home that enjoys God's blessing, and to make a plan to raise your children in the ways of God. This study is an invitation to Christian homebuilding, beginning with the sobering reality that our home and heart usually take the shape of our own character. Let's seek the Lord in the our homebuilding endeavor by taking a look at the landscape of our own lives.

###### Group Discussion

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-SeeGodWis-BibWis-GroDis-1">1. How would you describe your Christian faith and walk with the Lord? Would you describe yourself as a person of integrity?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-SeeGodWis-BibWis-GroDis-2">2. Who are people of integrity that you know? What makes them people of integrity? How does integrity lead to blessing on one's children?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-SeeGodWis-BibWis-GroDis-3">3. What hopes and dreams do you have for your children? What are God's desires for your children? Are these aligned?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-SeeGodWis-BibWis-GroDis-4">4. What areas of your life would need to change for you to better parent your children? What would it look like for your children to experience God's blessing?</Question>

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-SeeGodWis-BibWis-RecThoBel-1">###### Record Your Thoughts Below</Question>

#### Exploring the Biblical Text

*Study God's word together. Read the passage. Annotate the text and record your reflections on the following page. Answer the discussion questions, using the biblical commentary to help. Then, discuss the passage as a community.*

##### Scriptural Passage

<sup>2:21</sup> When the eight days until His circumcision had passed, He was named Jesus, the name the angel had given Him before He had been conceived. <sup>22</sup> And when the time of purification according to the Law of Moses was complete, His parents brought Him to Jerusalem to present Him to the Lord <sup>23</sup> (as it is written in the Law of the Lord: "Every firstborn male shall be consecrated to the Lord"), <sup>24</sup> and to offer the sacrifice specified in the Law of the Lord: "A pair of turtledoves or two young pigeons."

<sup>25</sup> Now there was a man in Jerusalem named Simeon, who was righteous and devout. He was waiting for the consolation of Israel, and the Holy Spirit was upon him. <sup>26</sup> The Holy Spirit had revealed to him that he would not see death before he had seen the Lord's Christ. <sup>27</sup> Led by the Spirit, he went into the temple courts. And when the parents brought in the child Jesus to do for Him what was customary under the Law, <sup>28</sup> Simeon took Him in his arms and blessed God, saying: <sup>29</sup> "Sovereign Lord, as You have promised, You now dismiss Your servant in peace. <sup>30</sup> For my eyes have seen Your salvation, <sup>31</sup> which You have prepared in the sight of all people, <sup>32</sup> a light for revelation to the Gentiles, and for glory to Your people Israel."

<sup>33</sup> The Child's father and mother were amazed at what was spoken about Him. <sup>34</sup> Then Simeon blessed them and said to His mother Mary: "Behold, this Child is appointed to cause the rise and fall of many in Israel, and to be a sign that will be spoken against, <sup>35</sup> so that the thoughts of many hearts will be revealed—and a sword will pierce your soul as well."

<sup>36</sup> There was also a prophetess named Anna, the daughter of Phanuel, of the tribe of Asher, who was well along in years. She had been married for seven years, <sup>37</sup> and then was a widow to the age of eightyfour. She never left the temple, but worshiped night and day, fasting and praying. <sup>38</sup> Coming forward at that moment, she gave thanks to God and spoke about the Child to all who were waiting for the redemption of Jerusalem. <sup>39</sup> When Jesus' parents had done everything *"And when the time of purification according to the Law of Moses was complete...*

required by the Law of the Lord, they returned to Galilee, to their own town of Nazareth. <sup>40</sup> And the Child grew and became strong. He was filled with wisdom, and the grace of God was upon Him.

<< Luke 2:21–40

##### Discussion Questions

###### Searching the Text

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeaTex-1">1. **Family Devotion**: How dedicated were Jesus' parents to God? What did Mary and Joseph do that showed their devotion to God?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeaTex-2">2. **Faith Community**: What do we learn about the the covenant community in the temple during the time of Jesus' dedication? What role can (and should) the community have in our parenting work?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeaTex-3">3. **God's Promises**: How does this narrative highlight the importance of God's promises being fulfilled in Christ? What does it look like to have a family that is established on God's promises? What role should the gospel have in our parenting?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeaTex-4">4. **Spiritual Growth**: How did Jesus grow and mature over the course of time? How can this provide an inspiring vision for our own children to grow in faith?</Question>

###### Seeking the Truth

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeeTru-1">1. **Family Devotion**: How does the health and godliness of a marriage impact parenting? How does the faith and godliness of each parent shape the family and children?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeeTru-2">2. **Faith Community**: Why is it important for families to be devoted to the life of the church? What happens to marriage and families when this does not take place?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeeTru-3">3. **God's Promises**: How important are God's promises for building Christian families? What role should the gospel have in our homes?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-SeeTru-4">4. **Spiritual Growth**: What responsibility do parents have to foster holistic development of their children? What are the blessings and challenges of this work?</Question>

###### Evaluating Our Lives

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-EvaLiv-1">1. **Family Devotion**: During your upbringing, how devoted was your home to the things of God? How devoted is your home now to the Lord?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-EvaLiv-2">2. **Faith Community**: How committed is your family to the church community? What obstacles make this a challenge? What blessings have your received from church community life?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-EvaLiv-3">3. **God's Promises**: How often do you speak about Christ and the gospel? How frequently do you remind your children of God's promises?</Question>
<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-Int-IntComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-EvaLiv-4">4. **Spiritual Growth**: How would you evaluate the spiritual growth of your children? What are areas to celebrate and to improve?</Question>

##### Dedicating Hearth and Home to God

> Then the Lord said to Moses, "Consecrate to Me every firstborn male. The firstborn from every womb among the Israelites belongs to Me, both of man and beast."

<< Exodus 13:1–2

> The business done in the home is nothing less than the shaping of the bodies and souls of humanity. The family is the factory that manufactures mankind.

<< G. K. Chesterton, "New Witness"

###### Introduction

Bearing children is a major milestone in life and faith. But *childbearing* is not the same as *childrearing*. The gift of each child born into the world is soon followed by a sobering sense of duty and responsibility to raise each child in a God-honoring way. All parents are accountable before God for how they raise their children. The weighty responsibility of this godly duty is balanced by the worthiness of the work. During the child-raising journey, parents experience deep joy and painful difficulty, they anticipate hopeful dreams and fearful uncertainties, and they feel initial excitement and eventual exhaustion. In our families, in the raising of our children, and in the cultivation of holy homes, we are enlisted in a great and glorious endeavor. <Callout>Through the family, society is shaped, the community is formed, the church is established, and the generations build God's kingdom.</Callout>

God has not left us without guidance in the sacred work of building Christian hearth and home. As with every other area of life, Christian parents look to Scripture to align their families and shape their parenting vocation after God's inspired plan. In Luke 2:21–40, Jesus is dedicated to God: the promised and eternal Son of God is dedicated to God the Father. As God-honoring parents, Mary and Joseph modeled faithful devotion and parenting foundations. They treasure baby Jesus most by offering him back to his heavenly Father. Not only does their example help us consider the work of parenting in general, but it calls us to the very specific duty of establishing our families—our marriages, our homes, our children—on the person and work of Christ. Every child is a gift and blessing from God, a gift best kept secure in God's safekeeping care. But Jesus is a special gift from God: God's one and only Son given to mankind for salvation. There is no better starting point to the parenting journey than to consider how to consecrate one's family and child to God, by laying a family foundation of Jesus Christ—the eternal Son of God who came into the world as a human baby. Let's look to Mary and Joseph as paradigmatic examples of this most noble task of building hearth and home devoted to the Lord.

###### Consecrated to the Lord: Establishing a Family Devoted to God

Soon after Jesus' birth, Mary and Joseph performed several faithful acts which dedicated the infant Christ to God: *circumcision*, *naming*, *presentation*, and *sacrifice*. As a Jewish practice, Jesus' circumcision established him within the Israelite covenant community. Naming Jesus was both an act of obedience—heeding the angelic message by giving him "the name the angel had given Him before He had been conceived" (2:21)—and expectation—anticipating Jesus' work of salvation. The journey to Jerusalem to "present Him to the Lord" (2:22) was a law-abiding act which "consecrated [Jesus] to the Lord" (2:23). The sacrificial offering of "a pair of turtledoves or two young pigeons" (2:23) took place "when the time of purification according to the Law of Moses was complete" (2:22; cf. Leviticus 12:1–8). This worshipful act signified atonement, cleansing, and removal of impurity. Emphatic in this biblical narrative is the devotion of Marry and Joseph to live "according to the Law" (2:22; cf. 2:21, 23–24, 27, 39). For the holy family, personal faith, marital priorities, and family life were aligned and submitted to God's word.

Although we are not bound by Jewish law, the faithfulness of Mary and Joseph provides an inspiring example to families welcoming children into the world. <Callout>Our very first acts as Christian parents should not be child-centric but God-focused.</Callout> That is, to mark one's home by worship, to consecrate our children to the Lord, to elevate the need for purification and atonement, to embed oneself in the community of faith, and to honor God's word as authoritative are fundamental foundations for all faithful families. Joseph and Mary tended to baby Jesus as stewards of a gracious gift given them by God. They realized that they could only best care for their child when they viewed that child as first belonging to God. Their firstborn son Jesus was first and foremost God's eternal only begotten Son. God had entrusted him to their temporary care. A family devotes itself to the Lord as it realizes that each and every child is a gift from God. And we raise our children as temporary caretakers of that which ultimately belongs to God.

The godliness of Joseph and Mary were paramount in shaping their home culture. Importantly, the godliness of one's marriage sets the tone for family culture and values. <Callout>Holiness and intimacy enjoyed between husband and wife irrigate the garden of the home.</Callout> But the vibrancy of a Christian marriage rests upon the individual devotion of each spouse to the Lord. As leader of the home, the father's piety is pacesetting—families most often go the way of the dad. The mother's godliness fosters a fertile environment for faith formation. The godliness of a home, including the maturity of husband and wife, is sustained by the church and contributes to the well-being of the Christian community. In reality, *child dedication* is a matter of *family consecration*: surrendering one's family wholly to God before the people of God. This wholehearted devotion takes place across several vectors: the child devoted to God, parents committing themselves to raise the child in the faith, and the church community committing themselves to the family and the childrearing process. Whether one has children or not, to be devoted to the Lord is to be dedicated to help all the children in the body of believers grow to full maturity. Families and churches mutually edifying each other in devotion to the Lord is the sweet symphony of being a part of the people of God.

###### Into the Temple Courts: Raising a Family Rooted in Faith Community

Even though Jesus was born when Mary was a virgin, both Mary and Joseph raised Jesus as his parents (2:33). The collaborative nature of parenting, between husband and wife, is reinforced by the loving support of a covenant community. Jesus' dedication in the temple (2:27, 37)—the gathering place of worship for God's people—signifies the important role of community in homebuilding. It was in temple that Jesus' parents met two godly individuals: Simeon and Anna. Simeon was "righteous and devout" (2:25). His life was surrendered to the Holy Spirit: "the Holy Spirit was upon him" (2:25), "the Holy Spirit had revealed to him" (2:26), and he was "led by the Spirit ... into the temple courts" (2:27). Anna lived a life characterized by spiritual service: she was "a prophetess" (2:36). Statements about her marriage (2:36), widowhood (2:37), and constant presence in the temple (2:37) highlight her godly character and uncompromising devotion to God. Her faith formation and maturity is emphasized by a myriad of spiritual practices: worship, fasting, prayer, and gratitude (2:37–38). Jesus' presentation in the temple, in the context of aged and godly saints, represents the important role the faith community plays in raising godly children.

No parent was meant to raise children alone. Not only is parenting the teamwork of husband and wife, but it is also the responsibility of the faith community. Children raised in a Christian church community mature as they are surrounded by older believers who take their faith seriously, practice spiritual habits, cling to God's promises, rehearse God's redemptive work, and and build their lives on God's saving promises. Seasoned believers in the faith community, like Simeon and Anna, can become a support to parents as they seek to raise their children in the discipline and instruction of the Lord. Godly examples of faithful believers who live a life worth emulating is one of the most powerful tools of faith formation.

<Callout>For all believers, to be devoted to God is to be dedicated to God's people. One cannot exist without the other.</Callout> There is a mutual interdependence that exists between families and churches. As a family of families, church maturity can often be measured by the health of its household units. Conversely, family maturity is reliant upon a strong faith community. The busyness of family life, the exhaustion of daily (and nightly) parenting duties, the difficulty of troubleshooting household dilemmas can easily keep us from the church. Many have foregone church fellowship in the name of caring for their families (cf. Hebrews 10:25). What a woeful and foolish decision! It is precisely in the church community that a household can blossom into a family of faith. For it is in the church where the larger purposes of God are worked out in his world and where God's people—including parents and children—get regularly acquainted with God's great and glorious promises to his people.

###### As You Have Promised: Fastening a Family Secure to God's Word

Parenting in community is fueled by divine promises. Jesus' dedication in the temple was more than just about the supportive influence of faith community. It was about the God who works to save his people and form the community of faith. Inasmuch as Simeon and Anna represent godly Christian community, they more fundamentally signify the hope of salvation. Simeon was "waiting for the consolation of Israel" (2:25) and Anna was "waiting for the redemption of Jerusalem" (2:38). Consolation and redemption are brought about by God's "salvation" (2:30), made possible by "the Lord's Christ" (2:26). The message of salvation—in fact, the culmination of all God's promises—is good news revealed about Jesus Christ (2:33, 36), the only one who save people from sin, judgment, and condemnation. This salvation God had "prepared in the sight of all people" (2:31). God made promises and came through on his promises so that salvation could become available for all people (2:31–32) who embrace Christ. All centers on Christ. He is the one who will reveal the thoughts of mankind (2:35). Jesus is the center upon which all people—individuals, families and communities—rise and fall (2:34).

In this narrative, obedience according to the Law (cf. 2:21–24, 27, 39) is buoyed up by faith that God does "as [he has] promised" (2:29). Christian homebuilding is an intentional setting of one's family on the rock of God's unalterable promises. And all of God's good promises are fulfilled in Christ. In fact, this narrative reminds all Christian homes are built on Christ. First, Simeon "blessed God" (2:29) for the Christ—an act of worship—which turned into a blessing of the holy family: "then Simeon blessed them" (2:34). The blessing of the home was connected to a heartfelt praise of God for the salvation that he had brought to fruition in Jesus. Consequently, Christian homebuilding must establish a gospel-centered, Christ-focused, salvation-oriented foundation. This is what child dedication, homebuilding, and family order is all about.

Parenting's main goal is for children to embrace Christ and experience his salvation. Homebuilding begins by a commitment to establish a household on Christ. In this story, Christ is more than an example of child dedication. He is the means by which salvation can come to our household. <Callout>A child dedicated in each Christian home must ultimately be devoted to the Christ child dedicated in Jerusalem.</Callout> When a home is dedicated to Christ, the family can bask in divine comfort, enduring peace, worship-filled awe, and everlasting joy. The first steps in ordering a home is submitting the home to Jesus as Savior and Lord.

###### And the Child Grew: Cultivating a Family as Context for Spiritual Growth

All child dedication and homebuilding rests on the hopeful gospel message announced during Christ's own dedication in the temple. Parenting is difficult and challenging work. Sometimes the parental duty can feel burdensome and overwhelming. Sometimes it can cause pain and heartache. This biblical text even testifies that Mary would experience pain as Jesus' mother: "a sword will pierce your soul as well" (2:35). Divine promises sustain us in the work. But God's promises in the gospel are not just for the life to come, but realities we can experience in this present life as well. In this way, the difficulty and labor of parenting should give birth to spiritual fruit and Christian maturity. In this narrative, Jesus' godly and faithful parents provided a rich context for faith formation (2:39). No doubt, their worship in the Jerusalem temple continued as faithful family formation in "their own town of Nazareth" (2:39). Over the course of time, Jesus "grew and became strong," he was "filled with wisdom," and "the grace of God was upon Him" (2:40). In fact, we learn that Jesus grew holistically across various domains of life (2:52).

Parents hope for growth in their children like the development Jesus experienced. When the gospel is the foundation of a home it provides a fertile context for spiritual formation. Godly parents hope for growth, strength, wisdom, and divine grace. To teach the gospel without instructing in godliness is a great fault of unbiblical parenting. Christian parents build their homes on Christ when their preaching of the gospel of Christ translates into instruction in the ways of Christ. Salvation is not possible outside of Jesus. If parents miss this simple point, all their efforts are vain and worthless. But no better pattern for godly living exists than Christ himself. Christ himself should be the curriculum of our home. Inasmuch as parents must keep the gospel at the center of their home, they must embody, exemplify, and commend Christlikeness as a formation goal. <Callout>Building home is like planting and cultivating a garden where seeds of infants and sprout, grow, blossom, and bear fruit for righteousness.</Callout>

Helpful metrics of godliness are outlined in Jesus' own development and formation. Although Jesus was fully God, he was also fully human (without sin). He experienced growth and maturity common to mankind. It is important to notice the holistic dimension of Jesus' growth. Over the course of his infancy, childhood, and youth, Jesus experienced physical, intellectual, moral, social, and spiritual development (2:40; cf. 2:52). This record of Jesus' development provides a helpful blueprint for Christian parents. With Jesus at the faith foundation of our families, we should uphold Jesus as the ultimate example by which we should shape our lives. Jesus is the means of salvation and the model of godliness. Our parenting should facilitate formation in all areas of life: establish faith in Christ, cultivate intimacy with God, promote holy relationships, instruct in biblical wisdom, foster physical well-being, encourage spiritual discipline, and stimulate moral living. But lest we think we discharge our parenting duty in human strength, we should remember that the key to the godly culture in the household of Joseph and Mary should be the key to our homes as well. The definitive mark of Jesus' life was that he was under the canopy of God's gracious favor. If we hold any hope for our families to be mature in godliness and our children to be courageous saints, it will be by the grace and favor of God. Let us pray that our children rest under the supernatural safety and enabling power of God's grace.

###### Conclusion

Jesus' dedication in the temple teaches us about parenting as much as it also recounts the gospel. Certainly, Christ's birth and dedication announce the good news of salvation to a lost and sinful world. But the events of this biblical narrative also help parents order their home and establish their family with Christ at the center. <Callout>Christian homebuilding is a matter of establishing our homes on the foundation of Christ.</Callout> Child dedication signifies that a home is serious about submitting their whole family to Jesus: the Christ child dedicated in the temple in Jerusalem. In sum, a Christian home is dedicated to God, rooted in Christian community, established on God's promises, and well-ordered to maximize spiritual growth. Taken seriously, these simple yet profound truths will help a family welcome its first children into the world (and into their family) in a way that seeks the child's best. And the child's best is a life fully offered to the Lord.

##### Session Summary

- Jesus's parents were fully devoted to the Lord and consecrated Jesus to God with wholehearted commitment and aligned their life and family according to God's word.
- The godliness of parents directly affects the godliness of the home. Godly parents make sure their individual walk with the Lord and their marriage is growing in Christlikeness.
- Godly families are rooted in Christian community because the community of God's people provides a rich context for spiritual growth through faith practices, godly examples, and corporate worship.
- Christian families build their homes on God's promises which are ultimately fulfilled in Christ. The good news of salvation in the gospel must serve as the foundation and focus of all parenting work.
- Christian homebuilding fosters a context in which children can grow in godliness and faithfulness to the Lord. Parents should see Jesus as the example for holistic spiritual growth.

#### Charting the Path Ahead

*Reflect on the session and envision next steps. Record key takeaways from the lesson. Conclude your time by praying as small groups and a whole community. Review next steps.*

##### Small Group Prayer

##### Corporate Prayer

Heavenly Father, thank you for sending your Son. He is the hope of the nations. He alone is the good news of salvation. He is the longed-for consolation for those suffering. He is the promise of enduring peace. He is the only comfort for the hurting. And he is the redemption of the world. Only he is the Savior of repentant sinners. May he be the foundation of our home, the vitality of our marriage, the focus of our family, and the source of eternal life to our children. Help us to embrace him like Simeon. Assist us to celebrate him like Anna. Give us your grace to build our family on Jesus, the Lord's Christ. We consecrate our children to you, dedicating these precious ones to Christ, the Promised One dedicated in Jerusalem. May our children believe in Christ as Savior and Lord. May they grow in Christlikeness of character on a daily basis. Help us as parents to model this way of life. Help us, O God! Amen!

- Complete the spiritual disciplines over the course of the week.

### Introduction Orientation and Overview

#### Orientation and Overview

*This section of the book helps to give you an overview of the rite of passage. It will summarize the main movements of the process and preview the main topics of the study. Use this section to orient yourself and your church community.*

##### Structure and Overview

###### Part One: Preparation

In the first part of this rite of passage, you will study six key roles of parents: *priests*, *stewards*, *shepherds*, *teachers*, *protectors*, and *overseers*. Each of these sessions is divided into three sections: a community-based study around the topic, spiritual disciplines for the intervening week, and reflective projects to apply and implement parenting principles in your family life. The expanded content of these topics is listed below.

###### Part Two: Challenge

The challenge phase of this rite of passage is a *family camp experience*. During this time, church leaders, mentors, and peers will enjoy God's creation as they meet with God, practice homesteading skills, and cultivate relationships with one another. A more developed outline of this time is sketched below.

###### Part Three: Celebration

The third part of this rite of passage concludes with a time of celebration. In this final movement in your entrance to the parenting, each parent will give a personal testimony of God's work in their life over the course of this process. This will also be a time for each parent (and family unit) to express their commitment to build Christian hearth and home for the Lord. The time ends with a ceremony in which the church community surrounds the parents and families with encouragement. The basic overview of this final phase is summarized below.

| Part 3: Celebration     |                                                                                 |  |
|-------------------------|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--|
| Personal<br>Testimony   | Reporting on God's work in your life.                                           |  |
| Community<br>Confession | Sharing your commitment to take responsibility in life and faith.               |  |
| Celebration<br>Ceremony | Concluding the rite of passage with a time of celebration and praise to<br>God. |  |

###### Part Four: Journal

The fourth part of this rite of passage is journal space for you to collect your thoughts, observations, and prayers along the way.

##### Mentoring and Community Roles

This rite of passage is best completed in the context of close relationships. Crossing into a new season of life is best facilitated with a network of trusted fellow travelers. In a Christian setting, these relationships can come from family, friendships, and the church community. *Church leaders* or *heads of household* help guide and direct this process, usually occupying the main teaching role. Going through this rite of passage with a group of *peers* can reinforce the ideas and provide a supportive community into the future. *Mentors* help encourage the participants along the way, helping them reflect on learnings, providing a structure for accountability, and promoting internalization of key biblical teachings. Usually, mentors have gone through the rite of passage or have already passed into the next season of life and are equipped to help encourage participants in their journey. The *family* and *church community* can help bolster the experience through orchestrating the experience, seeking to maximize the holistic and experiential essence of the rite of passage. Family and church are to be the main environment in which this rite of passage is worked out. *Couples* are encouraged to work through sessions individually and collectively. Here are some steps to make this rite of passage a rich and full-orbed experience.

- Identity trusted *church leaders* to teach and lead the main sessions.
- Pair up participants with *mentors*. Have mentors touch base with participants throughout the experience. Prepare mentors for their task throughout the main study sessions (Part One) and the mentoring sessions during the outdoor challenge experience (Part Two).
- Gather a group of *peers* who are going through the same experience together. While this rite of passage can be done individually, it will be an enriched experience with a community of peers.
- Marshall the extended *family network* and *church community* to help encourage, organize, and facilitate this experience. These groups can also help prepare for the time of celebration.

![The Milkmaid](vermeer_the_milkmaid)

<< Vermeer, Johannes. *The Milkmaid*. 1660. Oil on canvas, 45.5 × 41 cm. Rijksmuseum.

## Session One

#### Under God's Fatherly Care: Building a Home Devoted to God

##### Overview

> And when the days of feasting were over, Job would send for his children to purify them, rising early in the morning to offer burnt offerings for all of them. For Job thought, "Perhaps my children have sinned and cursed God in their hearts." This was Job's regular practice.

<< Job 1:5

> We may take great comfort from considering that, God stands to us in the relation of a Father ... He must from His very nature do everything in the best and most perfect manner, and in the fullest also; we may be sure He will be to us better than ever any earthly father has been to a son .... We have a Father in heaven; and He will not come short in the fulfilment of even one of the functions of a parent .... And because He is a Father you may expect all tender comfort.

<< Philip Bennet Power, *The Sick Man's Comfort Book*

The first session of this study explores the source of all godly parenting: God himself. Just like our Christian devotion to God is based upon God's unfailing commitment to us, so our parenting work is fueled, sustained, ordered, and empowered by our vibrant relationship with God as Father. To know and trust God as Father—to experience his divine compassion and tender love—is essential if we are to care for our children in the right way. After all, the whole aim of our parenting work is to initiate our children into their own relationship with God. Spiritual practices, household habits, and family worship all serve to establish our children in the faith to take ownership of their spiritual life, enjoying their own walk with the Lord. Our job as human parents is to encourage a relationship with God as Divine Parent. In this way, parents are like priests in their homes. Their sacred calling is to nurture children in the worship of God, a *most holy work* that begins with us fostering a *most humble walk* with the Lord.

##### Key Idea

As priests, we will nurture our children in God's fatherly care.

### Session 1 Community Study

#### Tuning Our Hearts to the Lord

*Unite your hearts together in worship. Use the community confession, corporate song, Scripture reading, responsive call, and opening prayer to enter God's presence together.*

##### Community Confession

By God's grace and through faith in Christ, our home belongs to the Lord. **As priests, we will nurture our children in God's fatherly care.** As stewards, we will pass on the faith of our fathers. As shepherds, we will lead our children as God shepherds us. As teachers, we will instruct our household in the fear of the Lord. As protectors, we will entrust our family to the God's safekeeping. As overseers, we will build Christian hearth and home as a heritage from the Lord. As for me and my house, we will serve the Lord. For God's glory, for our good, and for the sake of the world! Amen and amen!

##### Corporate Song

###### The Tender Love a Father Has

**Verse 1**
The tender love a father has For all his children dear, Such love the Lord bestows on them Who worship him in fear.

**Verse 2**
The Lord remembers we are dust, And all our frailty knows; Man's days are like the tender grass And as the flow'r he grows.

**Verse 3**
The flow'r is withered by the wind that smites with blighting breath; so man is quickly swept away before the blast of death.

**Verse 4**
Unchanging is the love of God, From age to age the same, Displayed to all who do his will And reverence his name.

**Verse 5**
Those who his gracious cov’nant  
keep  
The Lord will ever bless;  
Their children’s children shall rejoice  
To see his righteousness.

##### Scripture Reading

You, O Lord, are our Father; our Redeemer from Everlasting is Your name. Why, O Lord, do You make us stray from Your ways and harden our hearts from fearing You? Return, for the sake of Your servants, the tribes of Your heritage.

<< Isaiah 63:16

##### Responsive Call

**All**: Do not be terrified or afraid!

**Reader**: The Lord your God, who goes before you, will fight for you, just as you saw Him do for you in Egypt.

**All**: Do not be terrified or afraid!

**Reader**: The Lord your God carried you, as a man carries his son, all the way by which you traveled until you reached this place.

**All**: Do not be terrified or afraid!

<< Deuteronomy 1:31

##### Opening Prayer

Heavenly Father, we take comfort in your love and compassion. Your tender care is a refuge for us at all times. You look upon us in our need and mercifully come to our aid. You know our limits and daily provide us with just what we need. You are fully aware of our weakness and you grant us your own strength. You accept our confessions and receive us back with your forgiving embrace. Because of your grace, we are children of God. As your children, we are most loved and treasured. As we think about our parenting work, we are easily overwhelmed by the task. But we know you sustain us in what you call us to. We know our children belong to you more than they belong to us. As we serve our families as parents, help us to do so as children of God. May our children see in us a childlike faith that moves them to trust and adore you. Be to us our heavenly Father as we nurture our children in worship of God. Amen.

#### Seeking God's Wisdom

*Focus your thinking around the key idea and biblical wisdom. Review the introduction and discuss the scriptural proverb to help orient you to the main theme of this session.*

##### Introduction

> At that time Jesus declared, "I praise You, Father, Lord of heaven and earth, because You have hidden these things from the wise and learned, and revealed them to little children. Yes, Father, for this was well-pleasing in Your sight."

<< Matthew 11:25–26

> It is a sweet compound of faith that knows God to be my Father, love that loves him as my Father, joy that rejoices in him as my Father, fear that trembles to disobey him because he is my Father and a confident affection and trustfulness that relies upon him, and casts itself wholly upon him, because it knows by the infallible witness of the Holy Spirit, that Jehovah, the God of earth and heaven, is the Father of my heart.

<< Charles Spurgeon, "The Fatherhood of God"

God is a loving and caring Father to his children. As divine parent, God is not merely the exemplar of loving parenting work, he is its eternal source that energizes and enables godly parenting. We can only love our children toward God as we cultivate and sustain a vibrant relationship with God as Father. The childlike faith that defines our trust in our heavenly Father is the foundation and fuel for parenting our children in a godly way. Parents are God-appointed intermediaries—like priests—leading their home in the worship of God. Certainly, parenting work is epitomized by God the Father. But God is more than a parenting paradigm; God himself is the power, strength, and vitality each parent needs to be faithful in this most important work of rearing children. Godly parents can only build Christian hearth and home with the divine aid and loving presence of God the Father.

##### Biblical Wisdom

Trust in the Lord with all your heart, and lean not on your own understanding; in all your ways acknowledge Him, and He will make your paths straight.

<< Proverbs 3:5–6

###### Core Principle

The foundation of all parenting is our relationship with the Lord. Few things like the responsibility of childrearing can make us confront our limits, weakness, and inabilities. The invitation to parenting is a similar invitation to the life of faith, to "trust in the Lord with all your heart." Faith in God requires us to heed a second instruction: "lean not on your own understanding." Trust in God means we must divest trust in ourselves. However intelligent or educated a person may be, the work of childrearing will expose the unreliability of our own wisdom and the inadequacy of our skills. Parenting requires trust in God—a humble confidence in God in our efforts to build Christian hearth and home. God backs his instruction with good promises. If we fully depend upon God—"in all your ways acknowledge Him"—there is a hope that he will direct our steps—"He will make your paths straight." We are not alone in the parenting work. Not only because we have a faith community, but because we have a loving heavenly Father who is desirous to direct the every step of those who trust in him.

##### Scriptural Passage

<sup>103:1</sup> Bless the Lord, O my soul; all that is within me, bless His holy name. <sup>2</sup> Bless the Lord, O my soul, and do not forget all His kind deeds— <sup>3</sup> He who forgives all your iniquities and heals all your diseases, <sup>4</sup> who redeems your life from the Pit and crowns you with loving devotion and compassion, <sup>5</sup> who satisfies you with good things, so that your youth is renewed like the eagle's.

<sup>6</sup> The Lord executes righteousness and justice for all the oppressed. <sup>7</sup> He made known His ways to Moses, His deeds to the people of Israel. <sup>8</sup> The Lord is compassionate and gracious, slow to anger, abounding in loving devotion. <sup>9</sup> He will not always accuse us, nor harbor His anger forever. <sup>10</sup> He has not dealt with us according to our sins or repaid us according to our iniquities. <sup>11</sup> For as high as the heavens are above the earth, so great is His loving devotion for those who fear Him. <sup>12</sup> As far as the east is from the west, so far has He removed our transgressions from us. <sup>13</sup> As a father has compassion on his children, so the Lord has compassion on those who fear Him. <sup>14</sup> For He knows our frame; He is mindful that we are dust.

<sup>15</sup> As for man, his days are like grass—he blooms like a flower of the field; <sup>16</sup> when the wind passes over, it vanishes, and its place remembers it no more. <sup>17</sup> But from everlasting to everlasting the loving devotion of the Lord extends to those who fear Him, and His righteousness to their children's children— <sup>18</sup> to those who keep His covenant and remember to obey His precepts. <sup>19</sup> The Lord has established His throne in heaven, and His kingdom rules over all.

<sup>20</sup> Bless the Lord, all His angels mighty in strength who carry out His word, who hearken to the voice of His command. <sup>21</sup> Bless the Lord, all His hosts, you servants who do His will. <sup>22</sup> Bless the Lord, all His works in all places of His dominion. Bless the Lord, O my soul!

<< Psalm 103:1–22
