##### Opening Prayer

Overseeing God, we entrust ourselves to you, great Shepherd of the sheep. We are hungry: make us lie down in green pastures. We are thirsty: bring us to calm and refreshing waters. We are weary: grant us rest and restoration in our souls. We are prone to wander: lead us in the paths of righteous. We are weak: defend us from foe and give strength to our feeble frame. We are lost: rescue us from sin and safely bring us into your fold. The world is fierce and unforgiving, dangerous and depraved, perilous and corrupt. For us and for our family, carry us through the valley of the shadow of death. As we have received your all-wise and ever-good shepherding care, help us to tend to our children, caring for them the way you have cared for us. Make us faithful shepherds of our family flock. Help us, O God, we pray! Amen.

#### Seeking God's Wisdom

*Focus your thinking around the key idea and biblical wisdom. Review the introduction and discuss the scriptural proverb to help orient you to the main theme of this session.*

##### Introduction

> He will stand and shepherd His flock in the strength of the Lord, in the majestic name of the Lord His God. And they will dwell securely, for then His greatness will extend to the ends of the earth.

<< Micah 5:4

> Again, "Come near to me, Lord, since I am a man, and being a man am weak, and nothing can make me strong but thy presence. I am a man, so weak that if thou depart from me, I faint, I fall, I pine, I die; come near to me, then, O Lord, that by thy strength I may be encouraged and be fitted for service ... Come near me, then, my God, though I am so feeble, and as a tender parent feeds his child, and the shepherd carries his lambs, so come near to me."

<< Charles Spurgeon, "Peter's Prayer"

God is the Great Shepherd of his people. Showing his benevolent care, God provides for the needs of those who belong to him. With divine might and unsurpassable strength, God protects his people from predator and foe. Leading his people on the paths of righteousness, God disciplines and corrects. God demonstrates his love for his people in his tender care and loving supply. God nurtures, guides, secures, defends, and oversees his people as a diligent shepherd watches over his flock—all out of love and concern. Christian parents must care for their children the way that God tends to his people. When godly parents view their home as a flock, the can start to learn how to shepherd their children the way that God has shepherded them. In fact, parents best shepherd the flock of their families when they entrust themselves to God's shepherding care. Parents fulfill their shepherding role best when they assume their role as under-shepherds: God's representative caretakers assigned with the task of guiding their children under the gracious supervision of God's shepherding care.

##### Biblical Wisdom

Be sure to know the state of your flocks, and pay close attention to your herds.

<< Proverbs 27:23

###### Core Principle

Parenting work is like the work of a shepherd. A faithful shepherd provides for his sheep, defends them against enemies, leads them on safe paths, and corrects them when they err. A good shepherd shows his care for his sheep out of great love and genuine concern. But a loving shepherd is best able to care for his sheep when he knows what they need. This proverb calls us to "be sure to know the state of your flocks" and "pay close attention to your herds." Shepherds must be attentive to their flocks. Good shepherding care depends upon the ability of the shepherd to accurately and regularly know the condition of one's sheep. Likewise, parents must carefully attend to their children: understanding their needs, watching for danger, scoping the environment, evaluating their health, nurturing their growth, and admonishing them toward godliness. Parenting as shepherds requires this first great work: paying close attention to surroundings, knowing the needs of one's children, and discerning our family culture.

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-SesThr-Ses3ComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-EvaLiv-4">4. **Divine Shepherd**: How has God been a shepherd to you? How do you need God to be a shepherd to you now?</Question>

##### Shepherding Work in the Family Flock

> Know that the Lord is God. It is He who made us, and we are His; we are His people, and the sheep of His pasture.

<< Psalm 100:3

> Act lovingly to your children. In all your counsels and commands let them read love. Love will command honour; and how can a parent but love the child who is his living picture, nay, part of himself. The child is the father in the second edition.

<< Thomas Watson, *The Ten Commandments*

###### Introduction

Perhaps no context more shapes a person than the family setting. Family life is intensely formative. It is in the home that young children learn about faith, morality, relationships, values, work ethic, and life habits. During our earliest years of life, we are shaped by conversation, hobbies, humor, and shared experience in the home. The values upheld on the hearth eventually trickle into mainstream society. The influence of the family can go in two directions: leading children on the paths of righteousness or misguiding them on the road to destruction. <Callout>We often indirectly "pick up" as much from our family culture as we do from direct instruction.</Callout> Consequently, how we parent our children is as important as what we parent the in. The mode of parenting is as crucial as the content of our teaching and substance of our discipline.

Although many metaphors helpfully describe a parent's role and calling, one dominant biblical metaphor stands out to capture a godly mode of parenting. Throughout the Scripture, leaders are commonly referred to as shepherds—overseers who care for God's people like a herdsman tends to sheep. This term is used of Israel's rulers and church leaders. Even God himself describes his relationship to his people with shepherding terms. For this reason, we turn to Psalm 23:1–6 to aid us in our parental journey of Christian hearth-building and homebuilding. This psalm will help us learn basics of good shepherding in hopes to apply these skills in the way we care for our children. Ultimately, we hope to shepherd our children like God shepherds his people. Certainly, parents are not a replacement for God, but they are set up in their parental role as representatives of God. Because God relates to us as a shepherd, we should apprentice ourselves to his divine care. In the Lord as "my shepherd" we will surely find no better example.

###### The Lord Is My Shepherd: Exploring God's Shepherding Work

God rules with divine strength, righteous character, and loving concern. He is unrivaled in the way he cares for his own. God's people express their deep appreciation for and childlike trust in God's care with the words: "the Lord is my shepherd" (23:1). The shepherd image is quite vivid, depicting a vocation that involves oversight, care, guidance, and consistent supervision. God tends to his people as a shepherd watches over his flock—alert, attentive, tender, circumspect, and vigilant. Sheep depend upon the shepherd for their livelihood and safety. Shepherds feed, protect, lead, and defend their sheep, even at the cost of their own life. God as shepherd and believers as sheep are both fitting descriptions. What is noteworthy in the opening confession of this well-known psalm is the claim that God is "my" shepherd. This is a claim to personal relationship with the overseeing and caregiving God. God's shepherding care extends across his flock but is also tailored to the individual needs of each member of his covenant community. As a shepherd knows his sheep and sheep know their shepherd, so God is personally and relationally involved in caring for his people.

<Callout>Parenting work done in a shepherding mode emulates God himself—the Shepherd of shepherds.</Callout> Sheep are needy, weak, and vulnerable. Left to themselves they wander, stray, and get into danger. Without a shepherd to defend them, sheep are easy targets to predators. But sheep are most treasured by a loving shepherd. Parents need to be vigilant shepherds of their children, studying the way God cares for his own people. In parenting children is the work of providing for the needy, caring for the weak, defending the vulnerable, and guarding the flock from harmful predators. But godly parents must not only learn to shepherd as God shepherds; they first must be shepherded by God. In fact, a parent's ability to shepherd their children is directly related to their own submission to and receptivity of God's shepherding care in their own lives. We ever remain more fundamentally God's sheep than God's under-shepherding servants. Said another way, parents shepherd their children best when they are submissive sheep under God's faithful shepherding hand.

###### **Green Pastures and Quiet Waters: Enjoying God's Plentiful Provision and Guidance**

The opening of this psalm describes God as a shepherd who provides for his people. God's kindness and tender care are highlighted by the way he brings his people to "green pastures" and "quiet waters" (23:2). In these places, sheep can safely find food and drink. Green pastures bears witness to the fact that God supplies sufficiently for his people. Quiet waters attests to God sustaining his people in rest and repose. The result of God's provision is that the sheep "shall not want" (23:1). All their needs are met by the benevolent and open-handed shepherd. God holistically cares for his sheep —body and soul. In the psalm, God's shepherding care results in a refreshed and renewed inner being. God's provision causes the sheep to testify: "He restores my soul" (23:3). <Callout>God nurtures and uplifts, feeds and encourages, and nourishes and rejuvenates. He sustains body and fortifies soul.</Callout> But God's provision is more than kindhearted holistic supply, it is omniscient personalized care. God knows everything that his sheep really need and he is involved in their lives. God is the sovereign and unerring guide and director of the sheep's movements. The sheep testifies about the Lord: "He makes me lie down" (23:2), "He leads me" (23:2), and "He guides me" (23:3). God is able to amply and abundantly meet the needs of the sheep because he is an all-knowing and all-wise shepherd. Ultimately, the physical provisions for the sheep culminate into moral guidance: God guides his people "in the paths of righteousness for the sake of His name" (23:3). God's shepherding care expresses itself by leading his people to the best of all goods: God himself.

God appoints parents over the care of their children. Like a shepherd, parents are in a position to know more about the world and life than their children. From birth, infants are like sheep, utterly reliant upon their Godappointed caretakers. It can feel draining and depleting to constantly meet the needs of our children. The joy of parental work is providing for the needs of precious little humans who can offer us nothing in return. In this way, parenting demands a selfless spirit of constant sacrifice and outpouring generosity. Like faithful shepherds, loving parents put the needs of their children before their own comfort, convenience, or ease. But faithful parenting that lovingly and holistically meets the needs of our children is an honorable and joyful work. Christian homebuilding involves providing a place where needs are met—physical, emotional, relational, and spiritual. A home with parents who embrace shepherding care will communicate God's unmatched omnibenevolence by their thousand little acts of daily provision. As Christian hearth and home become a place of loving provision, they also become a place of nurture and formation—a place where trust is built, dependence is rewarded, faith is established, security is experienced, and goodness is embraced. Certainly, parents need to decipher the difference between a child's needs and wants. Parents want to give good things to their children, but they need wisdom so that their parental provision does not spoil the child. Nevertheless, parents always seek to offer their children what is best. In the end, this means guiding our children on a path where they can experience God's shepherding care for themselves.

###### Your Rod and Staff Comfort Me: Receiving God's Caring Protection and Correction

God's provision for his people includes his protection over them. God's loving supply is matched by his loyal strength. God is no weak overseer; he is an unmatched shepherd-warrior. God watches over his sheep with a guarding eye and mighty hand. He guards them from dangers without and threats within. He protects his sheep from enemies, and disciplines his sheep away from dangerous paths. The psalmist testifies about God's defending and discipling care: "Your rod and Your staff, they comfort me" (23:4). The rod is a weapon used to ward off predators; the staff is an instrument of guidance, correction, and leading. Together, these two shepherding tools represent God's protection and discipline. <Callout>Comfort is found in God's safeguarding rod and his corrective staff.</Callout> God uses his power to protect his people from enemies and to guide his people on the right path. Divine power applied in this way results in comfort. Sheep take refuge in God's power to defend them against foe and fiend, adversary and opponent. But sheep also come to love God's merciful correction as much as his mighty protection, because they know his powerfully guiding and corrective hand is keeping them from eventual danger.

Parental work is a balanced work of protection and correction. God-appointed shepherds over their home, parents safeguard their children from evil, defend against evildoers, shelter them in difficulty, and guard them in a violent and vicious world. Equally, parental work involves correction and discipline: warning about immorality, admonishing against bad relationships, rebuking falsehood, and confronting dangerous and deadly living. Protection and correction are are not antithetical to each other. To protect a vulnerable child is to demonstrate love and care. To correct an errant child is equally an expression of love and care. The rod in one hand and staff in the other is a faithful way to raise children. Godly discipline is a form of parental protection; parental protection can prove the loving intent of godly discipline. Certainly, parents can be overprotective—coddling children to the point where they face no difficulty or danger and leaves them unprepared for life. But parents can also forsake their protective duty, offering up children to a vulturine culture and society. To shepherd as God shepherds us, we must bear both rod and staff toward comforting ends.

###### You Are With Me: Experiencing God's Peace-Giving Presence and Nearness

Divine protection does not mean that God keeps his people from all danger and difficulty. This psalm assumes that sheep will find themselves in harrowing circumstances. When sheep enter difficulty and danger, God's presence is their enduring peace. Even when God's sheep "walk through the valley of the shadow of death" (23:4), they can enjoy rest and repose. The constant refrain of the God's people is a confession of trust in God's immanent presence: "You are with me" (23:4). The presence of God overpowers all menacing threats, allowing God's people to bear witness: "I will fear no evil" (23:4). Protection does not mean avoiding all difficulty, but seeing how God provides in the midst of surrounding enemies. In fact, God's people testify that God will "prepare a table before me in the presence of my enemies" (23:5).

As God's presence offers peace to his people, parental presence is a mainstay for their home. <Callout>Our faithful presence with our children can be one of our greatest ministry modes to our children.</Callout> Children thrive by healthy attachment and bonds with their parents. Healthy bonds with parents in the younger years can best prepare children more their more independent release into the world. As children build their trust in parents who faithfully shepherd them, the hope is that the children are learning to trust ultimately in God. There is a distorted version of reliance upon parents—a codependence in which parents care for their children for their own sense of self-worth, self-esteem, and selfsignificance. In this malformed view of parenting, parents care for their children for the benefit it brings back to themselves as parents. If not careful, this unbiblical mode of parenting can create entitlement, spoil character, and stifle faith. But there is also an unhealthy absenteeism in parenting, where parents are not involved and not present with their children in meaningful and healthy ways. Sometimes parents hand over the responsibility of parenting to others in the community, forsaking their primary duty to shepherd their children. No person is in a better spot to influence their children than the parents. We must avoid self-serving codependence and irresponsible absenteeism.

Much is at stake in our parenting venture. <Callout>In many ways, parenting amounts to preparing our children to live faithfully in a dangerous world a world rife with dark valleys, deadly shadows, evil forces, and hostile enemies.</Callout> Faithful presence with our children helps them learn how to face difficulty with hope in God. As shepherds, we walk with our children in painful valleys so that they are prepared to endure suffering for themselves. Daily, we commune in the presence of God so that our children can learn to trust God and find victory over death itself. As a family, we live faithfully in a sinful world—embracing countercultural values in our home, teaching our children how to walk with God, and showing them through our example how to fear no evil. We feast on God's bountiful provision while enemies surround, adversaries malign, foes ridicule, and opponents intimidate us. All the while, we do not flinch at their threats. We prepare our children for this dangerous and deadly world by entrusting them to God's shepherding care.

###### I Will Dwell in the House of the Lord Forever: Looking Ahead to God's Future Promise

God shepherds his people toward future promise. The end of the psalm expresses confidence that God's people will eat a banquet even though their enemies surround them (23:5). In this banquet, God's people enjoy his blessing, signified by the phrase, "You anoint my head with oil" (23:5). The blessings God provides for his people are promised to be abundant: "my cup overflows" (23:5). But God's banquet feast is more than physical food and drink (cf. 23:1). God promises that he will bountifully supply for his people, giving them a continuous supply of unending good: "surely goodness and mercy will follow me all the days of my life" (23:6). But the our shepherding Lord promises even more: eternal fellowship with God for all time. The climactic conclusion of this psalm bears witness to this promised reality of eternal life with God: "I will dwell in the house of the Lord forever" (23:6). <Callout>A faithful shepherd, God leads his people securely from this life into the world to come.</Callout>

Godly parenting is grounded upon God's promises. Faithful parent-shepherds orient their children to God's blessing, training their appetites to long for all that is truly good, beautiful, and right. Certainly, some of God's promises are experienced during out brief earthly lives. In response to these provisions, God's people offer up thanks and praise to God. But some of God's promises will only come to fruition in the future: eternal life, unending fellowship with God, final defeat of enemies, removal of sin and unrighteousness, and defeat of the shadow of death. When parents discharge their parental duty as faithful shepherds, they calibrate their family culture to God's good and trustworthy promises. As Christians, a healthy family culture will yield the fruit of godly virtue when it longs for God's blessing and promises above all else. Christian homebuilding involves celebration of God's faithfulness, thanksgiving for timely provision, praise for undeserved blessing, expectation for bountiful supply, and hope for full life of unmixed good and world without end. No better parenting plan exists than to build heart and home on the foundation of divine promise and unmerited blessing.

###### Conclusion

Godly parents follow God's example as a faithful and loving shepherd. But godly parents first recognize that their shepherding capacity to give care to their children is directly dependent upon them accepting God's shepherding care in their own lives. We can only give to others what we first receive from God. As faith-filled sheep we faithfully shepherd our children. In this way, God's shepherding example becomes a most helpful and holy paradigm for parenting. In our work of provision, our vocation of leading, our duty of protection, and our ministry of presence, may be emulate the strong and kind shepherding care we have experienced from God in our our own lives.

##### Session Summary

- Parents are primary shepherds of their children and should care for their children the way God cares for his people.

- As shepherds, parents should provide, guide, protect, and correct their children with love. As shepherds are present with their flocks, so parents should minister to their children with faithful presence.
- Parents shepherd their children by leading them into God's promises—certain hope of a blessed future.
- Parents shepherd best when they receive God's shepherd care in their own lives.

#### Charting the Path Ahead

##### Final Review

*Reflect on the session and envision next steps. Record key takeaways from the lesson. Conclude your time by praying as small groups and a whole community. Review next steps.*

##### Small Group Prayer

##### Corporate Prayer

Almighty God, you have shepherded your people throughout the generations. We are grateful to be numbered among your flock. Our blessings is in our belonging to you. In your grace, you sought and rescued us. In your goodness, you have provided for our daily needs. In your mercy, you have refreshed our souls. In your power, you have treaded on our foes. In your patience, you have kindly led us on the righteous paths. We are unable to shepherd our children in our own strength. Our failings are too great. Our strength is too feeble. Our hearts are too weak. We are only under-shepherds to this great work of parenting care. We can only shepherd them as you continue to daily shepherd us. Help us build Christian hearth and home. Make our home a place of green pastures and still waters, eternal life and righteous ways, loving correction and tender comfort, unending goodness and divine mercy. Help us, O God! Amen!

##### Verse Collection

*Prayerfully read through the selected passages of Scripture, identifying important truths they teach. Mark verses that you find encouraging.*

**Psalm 28:8–9**

The Lord is the strength of His people, a stronghold of salvation for His anointed. Save Your people and bless Your inheritance; shepherd them and carry them forever.

**Psalm 78:70–72**

He chose David His servant and took him from the sheepfolds; from tending the ewes He brought him to be shepherd of His people Jacob, of Israel His inheritance. So David shepherded them with integrity of heart and guided them with skillful hand

**Isaiah 40:10–11**

Behold, the Lord God comes with might, and His arm establishes His rule. His reward is with Him, and His recompense accompanies Him. He tends His flock like a shepherd; He gathers the lambs in His arms and carries them close to His heart. He gently leads the nursing ewes.

**Jeremiah 3:15**

"Return, O faithless children," declares the Lord, "for I am your master, and I will take you—one from a city and two from a family—and bring you to Zion.Then I will give you shepherds after My own heart, who will feed you with knowledge and understanding."

**Jeremiah 23:1–4**

Woe to the shepherds who destroy and scatter the sheep of My pasture!" declares the Lord. Therefore this is what the Lord, the God of Israel, says about the shepherds who tend My people: "You have scattered My flock and driven them away, and have not attended to them. Behold, I will attend to you for the evil of your deeds, declares the Lord. Then I Myself will gather the remnant of My flock from all the lands to which I have banished them, and I will return them to their pasture, where they will be fruitful and multiply. I will raise up shepherds over them who will tend them, and they will no longer be afraid or dismayed, nor will any go missing, declares the Lord.

**Ezekiel 34:2–6**

Woe to the shepherds of Israel, who only feed themselves! Should not the shepherds feed their flock? You eat the fat, wear the wool, and butcher the fattened sheep, but you do not feed the flock. You have not strengthened the weak, healed the sick, bound up the injured, brought back the strays, or searched for the lost. Instead, you have ruled them with violence and cruelty. They were scattered for lack of a shepherd, and when they were scattered they became food for all the wild beasts. My flock went astray on all the mountains and every high hill. They were scattered over the face of all the earth, with no one to search for them or seek them out.'

**Ezekiel 34:11–16**

For this is what the Lord God says: 'Behold, I Myself will search for My flock and seek them out. As a shepherd looks for his scattered sheep when he is among the flock, so I will look for My flock. I will rescue them from all the places to which they were scattered on a day of clouds and darkness. I will bring them out from the peoples,

> gather them from the countries, and bring them into their own land. I will feed them on the mountains of Israel, in the ravines, and in all the settlements of the land. I will feed them in good pasture, and the lofty mountains of Israel will be their grazing land. There they will lie down in a good grazing land; they will feed in rich pasture on the mountains of Israel. I will tend My flock and make them lie down, declares the Lord God. I will seek the lost, bring back the strays, bind up the broken, and strengthen the weak; but the sleek and strong I will destroy. I will shepherd them with justice.'

**John 10:11–15**

I am the good shepherd. The good shepherd lays down His life for the sheep. The hired hand is not the shepherd, and the sheep are not his own. When he sees the wolf coming, he abandons the sheep and runs away. Then the wolf pounces on them and scatters the flock. The man runs away because he is a hired servant and is unconcerned for the sheep. I am the good shepherd. I know My sheep and My sheep know Me, just as the Father knows Me and I know the Father. And I lay down My life for the sheep.

**1 Peter 5:1–4**

As a fellow elder, a witness of Christ's sufferings, and a partaker of the glory to be revealed, I appeal to the elders among you: Be shepherds of God's flock that is among you, watching over theme not out of compulsion, but because it is God's will; not out of greed, but out of eagerness; not lording it over those entrusted to you, but being examples to the flock. And when the Chief Shepherd appears, you will receive the crown of glory that will never fade away.

**Acts 20:28–29**

Keep watch over yourselves and the entire flock of which the Holy Spirit has made you overseers. Be shepherds of the church of God, which He purchased with His own blood. I know that after my departure, savage wolves will come in among you and will not spare the flock.

**Matthew 9:35–36**

Jesus went through all the towns and villages, teaching in their synagogues, preaching the gospel of the kingdom, and healing every disease and sickness. When He saw the crowds, He was moved with compassion for them, because they were harassed and helpless, like sheep without a shepherd.

**John 21:15–17**

When they had finished eating, Jesus asked Simon Peter, "Simon son of John, do you love Me more than these?" "Yes, Lord," he answered, "You know I love You." Jesus replied, "Feed My lambs." Jesus asked a second time,"Simon son of John, do you love Me?" "Yes, Lord," he answered, "You know I love You." Jesus told him, "Shepherd My sheep." Jesus asked a third time, "Simon son of John, do you love Me?" Peter was deeply hurt that Jesus had asked him a third time, "Do you love Me?" "Lord, You know all things," he replied. "You know I love You." Jesus said to him, "Feed My sheep."

#### Spiritual Practice

*Use the following activity as a concrete way to commune with the Lord. Set aside enough time to complete this activity in its entirety, following each step as a guide.*

##### Getting Quiet Before God

*Read the following verse out loud. Then, spend several minutes in silence before the Lord, acknowledging his presence.*

> I will surely gather all of you, O Jacob; I will collect the remnant of Israel. I will bring them together like sheep in a pen, like a flock in the midst of its pasture—a noisy throng. One who breaks open the way will go up before them; they will break through the gate, and go out by it. Their King will pass through before them, the Lord as their leader.

<< Micah 2:12–13

##### Acknowledging God as Shepherd

*After several minutes of silence, offer this prayer to God. Pray with open hands facing upwards to symbolize you stand before God in complete openness.*

> God, we know you are gathering your flock from among the peoples. You are establishing your people across the nations. You are establishing your testimony throughout the world. In our home, we can participate in your great work, shepherding our children into your shepherding care. Help us to take this job seriously. Assist us to fulfill this work faithfully. Empower us to discharge our duty lovingly. Make us fit shepherds of our family flock. To the glory and praise of God! Amen.

##### Defining Home Values

*Go through every room of your house and take a walk around the outside of your house. As you visit each room (i.e., kitchen, living room, bedroom, etc.), prayerfully consider what currently defines your family values and what values you want to characterize your home. As you walk outside of your house, prayerfully reflect on what kind of testimony you want your home to have in the surrounding world.*

He heals the brokenhearted and binds up their wounds. He determines the number of the stars; He calls them each by name. Great is our Lord, and mighty in power; His understanding has no limit. The Lord sustains the humble, but casts the wicked to the ground.

<< Psalm 147:1–6

### Session 3 Reflective Projects

#### Personal Project

*The following activity helps you internalize the biblical teaching and develop theological wisdom for parenting by grappling with the implications of the teaching in your own life.*

##### Project Instructions

Consider how parenting is shepherding work. Write a job description for parenting that describes it in shepherding language. Consider the challenges that will be faced, the skills that will be needed, the motivations that will be essential, and the outcomes that will be hoped for.

##### Example Job Description

- As a shepherd, I am tasked to lead and guide my children. I will steer them to the timeless truth of God's word and to the paths of righteous living.
- As a shepherd, I am called to feed and nourish my family. I will study God's word so that I can teach and nurture my family on your eternal revelation. I will provide for my children in body and spirit, keeping them from unhealthy teaching and falsehood.
- As a shepherd, I am called to protect and guard my family. I will be a sentry to my home, being watchful of what is happening in surrounding culture. I will not let evil and wickedness infiltrate our walls or ears or hearts. I will be vigilant and wise with technology, cautious to not unleash the digital world prematurely upon my children, teaching them how to use it wisely, and modeling this in my own life.
- As a shepherd, I will minister and care for my family with my faithful presence. I will be around the family and involved in their lives. I will study each of my children personally, so that I know their heart and they know my voice.
- As a shepherd, I will place our home under the safekeeping of Christ—the Good Shepherd. I will teach them that the safest and most blessed life is to be one of God's flock, fully entrusted to Jesus' care.

##### Shepherding Job Description

#### Parenting Project

*The role of a parent is to cultivate a home that worships God. We can nurture devotion to God as we practice faithful habits of family worship. Below is your opportunity to come up with a plan for family worship.*

##### Discuss with Your Spouse

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-SesThr-Ses3RefPro-ParPro-DisSpo-1">- **Reflecting on Your Past**: How would you describe the culture of your home during your upbringing? What were the values of your home?</Question>

## Session Four

#### In the Fear of God: Instructing Children in Godliness

##### Overview

> Listen, my son, and receive my words, and the years of your life will be many. I will guide you in the way of wisdom; I will lead you on straight paths.

<< Proverbs 4:10–11

> First, then, if you would train your children rightly, train them in the way they should go, and not in the way that they would .... If, then, you would deal wisely with your child, you must not leave him to the guidance of his own will. Think for him, judge for him, act for him, just as you would for one weak and blind; but for pity's sake, give him not up to his own wayward tastes and inclinations. It must not be his likings and wishes that are consulted. He knows not yet what is good for his mind and soul, any more than what is good for his body. You do not let him decide what he shall eat, and what he shall drink, and how he shall be clothed. Be consistent, and deal with his mind in like manner. Train him in the way that is scriptural and right, and not in the way that he fancies.

<< J. C. Ryle, *How Should a Child Be Trained?*

In this session, we explore the role of parent as teacher. Infants are born into the world without a well-formed understanding. They need to be taught. Children do not inherently perceive danger, grasp truth, or walk in righteousness. They need instruction, guidance, teaching, and direction. Youthful naiveté is initially inevitable and proves the need for education. Parental education is more than transfer of information; it is instruction in wisdom, discernment, and prudence. To gain wisdom is to gain skill in life, navigating the complexities of the world with God-honoring orientation. To educate in wisdom is to teach children what is true, good, beautiful, and right—leading them in a path of moral formation and training them to love what is holy. At its core, character development is rooted in the fear of the Lord. If we could summarize the underlying aim of all parenting work, it is this most simple yet demanding task: *instruct children in the fear of the Lord*. When deeply rooted in godly fear, children can grow up to be wise in the face of difficulty, godly when surrounded by evil, just in the midst of injustice, and faithful when experiencing suffering.

##### Key Idea

As teachers, we will instruct our household in the fear of the Lord.

### Session 4 Community Study

#### Tuning Our Hearts to the Lord

*Unite your hearts together in worship. Use the community confession, corporate song, Scripture reading, responsive call, and opening prayer to enter God's presence together.*

##### Community Confession

By God's grace and through faith in Christ, our home belongs to the Lord. As priests, we will nurture our children in God's fatherly care. As stewards, we will pass on the faith of our fathers. As shepherds, we will lead our children as God shepherds us. **As teachers, we will instruct our household in the fear of the Lord.** As protectors, we will entrust our family to the God's safekeeping. As overseers, we will build Christian hearth and home as a heritage from the Lord. As for me and my house, we will serve the Lord. For God's glory, for our good, and for the sake of the world! Amen and amen!

##### Scripture Reading

Teach me Your way, O Lord, that I may walk in Your truth. Give me an undivided heart, that I may fear Your name. I will praise You, O Lord my God, with all my heart; I will glorify Your name forever.

<< Psalm 86:11–12

##### Responsive Call

**Reader**: Let all the earth fear the Lord; let all the people of the world revere Him.

**All**: The fear of the Lord is a fountain of life, turning a man from the snares of death.

Proverbs 8:13; Psalm 111:10; Psalm 33:8; Proverbs 14:27

##### Opening Prayer

Good Lord, you are the epitome of kindness and the pinnacle of joy. All that is righteous, pure, satisfying, and delightful is found in you alone. Worldly dainties have nothing to offer. Sinful pleasures cannot enrich or assuage our empty, pining hearts. Quench our thirst. Satiate our hunger. Fulfill our longings. Satisfy in us all that is inconsolable. Help us to taste and see that the Lord is unmixed Good. Make our home a place that magnifies your glory, celebrates your goodness, enjoys your presence, and bears witness to your greatness. May we instruct our children in the fear of the Lord, the only sure foundation for joy and bliss. May the fear of God overrule and overcome all base and baseless fears. Build a Christ-centered hearth and home that follows you in genuine faith, heartfelt honor, and wholehearted obedience. Amen.

#### Seeking God's Wisdom

*Focus your thinking around the key idea and biblical wisdom. Review the introduction and discuss the scriptural proverb to help orient you to the main theme of this session.*

##### Introduction

> And now, O Israel, what does the Lord your God ask of you but to fear the Lord your God by walking in all His ways, to love Him, to serve the Lord your God with all your heart and with all your soul, and to keep the commandments and statutes of the Lord that I am giving you this day for your own good?

<< Deuteronomy 10:12–13

> What children are to be at a more advanced age, depends on the character they form in childhood. There is no subject of more momentous interest to the world, therefore, than the efforts of parents to educate them in the fear of God ... In a few years, our sons will become the agents in all that is interesting to the church and the world, and our daughters every where exerting a happy, or baleful influence throughout human society. Our sun begins to set, theirs just begins to rise. Before we are aware of it, we shall be in our graves, or laid aside as useless, and our children occupying our places of labor and business, and exerting our influence and authority .... If our children are educated in the fear of God, the happy influence of their moral training will be extended through a long line of descendants, and be diffused far and wide over the earth.

<< Gardiner Spring, *Hints to Parents*

The ultimate aim of parenting is to nurture children in the fear of the Lord. Only with this most vital foundation in genuine faith, children can experience true moral formation. When the fear of God is sown in the heart of the child, the core character of the youth undergoes a radical and dynamic change. They become new creatures in the Lord, orienting their conduct, speech, thoughts, desires, intentions, and relationships to what pleases God. When children grow in Christian character, their change of life is more than external modification of behavior, a change which does not weather difficulty or hardship. Genuine growth in godly character—that is, the cultivation of Christian virtue—is a substantive and lasting change. Those who fear the Lord can endure hardship, experience loss, face persecution, undergo disappointment, and suffer unmentionable ordeals with an abiding trust in God. What better aim of parenting can exist: *to raise children who relinquish their autonomy, revere God, relish his goodness, rest in his promises, and reform their life based on what pleases God*. To build Christian hearth and home in the fear of the Lord—a fear that rests upon tasting the unalloyed goodness of God—is to raise children as agents of transformation in the surrounding world.

##### Biblical Wisdom

Train up a child in the way he should go, and when he is old he will not depart from it.

<< Proverbs 22:6

###### Core Principle

The parenting vocation is a teaching work. God appoints all parents to be primary teachers of their children. The proverb pointedly commands us, "train up a child in the way he should go." The nuance to this instruction is important. First, training requires more than periodic lectures or verbal warnings. The best training is truth put into practice in daily life and modeled by the worthy teacher. Second, parents are to train their children in the "way he should go." There is a way that children want to go. The natural bent of children is to sin, selfishness, and destruction. The way he should go is a path of faith, self-surrender, and righteousness. Parents are the primary agents of education for their children. While there are many topics of education that are important to explore, a parent's most important work is to introduce children to faith in God. This proverb holds a promise along with its command: "when he is old he will not depart from it." This promise is not an absolute guarantee of a child's outcome. Many children receive godly training and walk away from the faith. Ultimately, children are responsible to apply parental teaching to their lives. Nevertheless, this proverb's promise summarizes a general truth: raise children in God's ways will have a lasting effect on their lives.

##### Scriptural Passage

<sup>34:1</sup> I will bless the Lord at all times; His praise will always be on my lips. <sup>2</sup> My soul boasts in the Lord; let the oppressed hear and rejoice. <sup>3</sup> Magnify the Lord with me; let us exalt His name together. <sup>4</sup> I sought the Lord, and He answered me; He delivered me from all my fears. <sup>5</sup> Those who look to Him are radiant with joy; their faces shall never be ashamed. <sup>6</sup> This poor man called out, and the Lord heard him; He saved him from all his troubles.

<sup>7</sup> The angel of the Lord encamps around those who fear Him, and he delivers them. <sup>8</sup> Taste and see that the Lord is good; blessed is the man who takes refuge in Him! <sup>9</sup> Fear the Lord, you His saints, for those who fear Him lack nothing. <sup>10</sup> Young lions go lacking and hungry, but those who seek the Lord lack no good thing. <sup>11</sup> Come, children, listen to me; I will teach you the fear of the Lord. <sup>12</sup> Who is the man who delights in life, who desires to see good days? <sup>13</sup> Keep your tongue from evil and your lips from deceitful speech. <sup>14</sup> Turn away from evil and do good; seek peace and pursue it.

<sup>15</sup> The eyes of the Lord are on the righteous, and His ears are inclined to their cry. <sup>16</sup> But the face of the Lord is against those who do evil, to wipe out all memory of them from the earth. <sup>17</sup> The righteous cry out, and the Lord hears; He delivers them from all their troubles. <sup>18</sup> The Lord is near to the brokenhearted; He saves the contrite in spirit. <sup>19</sup> Many are the afflictions of the righteous, but the Lord delivers him from them all. <sup>20</sup> He protects all his bones; not one of them will be broken. <sup>21</sup> Evil will slay the wicked, and the haters of the righteous will be condemned. <sup>22</sup> The Lord redeems His servants, and none who take refuge in Him will be condemned.

<< Psalm 34:1–22

###### Evaluating Our Lives

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-SesFou-Ses4ComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-EvaLiv-1">1. **Worship**: What actions can foster your home in the worship of God? How can you build your home to be a continual place of worship?</Question>

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-SesFou-Ses4ComStu-ExpBibTex-DisQue-EvaLiv-4">4. **Righteous Suffering**: Are your children being prepared to face difficulty and hardship by maintaining a faith in God? How can you orient your parenting toward this goal?</Question>

##### Forming Faith in the Fear of the Lord

> The fear of the Lord leads to life, that one may rest content, without visitation from harm.

<< Proverbs 19:23

> No man more truly loves God than he that is most fearful to offend Him ... Lord, who can know you and not love you, know you and not fear you; fear you for your justice and love you for your mercy; yes, fear you for your mercy and love you for your justice, for you are infinitely good in both.

<< Thomas Adams, *Works*

###### Introduction

As we seek to build our home on biblical and God-honoring foundations, we realize that homes are more than brick and mortar. The household that occupies the house is far more important is our work of Christian homebuilding. This means that our work of parenting involves focused work on our children growing into godly men and women. <Callout>At its core, parenting is a work of faith development and moral formation.</Callout> What we are seeking is much different than mere management of a child's external conduct. We are seeking spiritual change at the level of character and virtue. As we will learn, at the foundation of lasting spiritual transformation is the fear of the Lord. Consequently, we turn to Psalm 34:1–22, a passage invites children to come and learn the fear of the Lord. When the fear of God is at the foundation of a home, we can cultivate an environment where our children can come to experience and embrace God for themselves in genuine faith. Only as this happens, can we hope our children to be formed in godly character, a righteous character that comes from love of the glory of God, delight in the goodness of God, experience of the grace of God, and hope in the promise of God.

**At All Times:**

**Building Worship Habits to Live in God's Presence**

The psalm opens with a call to worship. The terms in this passage highlight what a life of worship entails: blessing (34:1), praising (34:1), boasting (34:2), rejoicing (34:2), magnifying (34:3), and exalting (34:3). Specifically, this psalm offers a call to continual doxology: unceasing worship of God "at all times" and "always be on my lips" (34:1). It is also a call for corporate worship—to praise God "with me" and to exalt God "together" (34:1). To continually and corporately worship God is to find remedy to life's challenges. This is why the humble, lowly, poor, needy, afraid, and distressed are invited to worship God (34:2, 4, 6). Communing in God's presence with a spirit of praise and a heart of worship is treatment to life's troubles.

This psalm's introduction is yet another reminder that worship is at the heart of the Christian life and should be at the foundation of all homebuilding. When a family consecrates itself to the Lord, it sets itself apart for one all-consuming purpose: exalting in the glory and goodness of God. Fathers and mothers will steward their parenting role best to the extent they prioritize the collective life of worship in their family. The appeal for continual worship in this psalm is instructing. Certainly, it is a call to develop habits of worship. <Callout>Parents serve their family by cultivating habits of worship early on in the lives of their children.</Callout> But designated times of family worship are not the only ways to cultivate habits of worship. Families can express God's praise all the time. During the thousand menial tasks and unending daily routines, we can let the praise of God "always be on my lips" (34:1). We Over time, the worship of God in ordinary and special moments builds a consciousness of God, teaching our children how to continually live in the presence of God.

###### At All Times: Building Worship Habits to Live in God’s Presence

###### Come, Children, Listen to Me: Calling Our Children to the Fear of God

A life of worship is fueled by the fear of God. At the center of this psalm and at the center of the Christian life—is a healthy and holy fear of the Lord. The psalmist describes what happens to "those who fear Him" (34:7; cf. 34:9), enjoins the saints to "fear the Lord" (34:9), and summons children to be instructed in "the fear of the Lord" (34:11). Importantly, this summons is given to children: "Come, children, listen to me; I will teach you the fear of the Lord" (34:11). The fear of the Lord is a synonym for genuine faith. When people genuinely fear God, they demonstrate faith in God by seeking him (34:4, 10), looking to him (34:5), calling out to him (34:6), and take refuge in him (34:8). Genuine faith as fear of the Lord results in divine aid (34:4), deliverance (34:4, 7), radiant joy (34:5), freedom from shame (34:5), gracious salvation (34:6), protection (34:7), blessing (34:8), and bountiful provision (34:9–10). Amazingly, when we have a fear of God, we can be delivered from all our other fears (34:4). Far from a fear that repels, the fear of the Lord is that which attracts and draws us toward the Lord. Those who fear God are those who "taste and see that the Lord is good" (34:9). They honor God in his holiness, revere God in his majesty, take God seriously for judgment, and cherish God for his goodness.

The thematic repetition in this passage not only helps us pinpoint a major biblical theme or a vital element of Christian living, but also helps focus our parenting work on a most essential foundation. At its core, the parental vocation teaches children the fear of the Lord—a summary of biblical faith. The fear of God involves awe of God's beauty, esteem of God's glory, terror at God's wrath, submission to God's authority, and trust in God's goodness. From infancy and childhood to adolescence and young adulthood, parents are key in helping children navigate their fears—learning to discern what is appropriate fear and fending off irrational and baseless fears. Healthy and holy fear of God will vanquish every other lesser fear. It will make a life whole and sturdy, fit to endure great difficulty without abandoning loyalty to God.

So much of life is determined by what we fear. And learning to fear God rightly is of utmost importance in our home. But faith-building by teaching the fear of God is not fear-mongering manipulation or authoritarian intimidation. Inasmuch as the fear of the Lord deters us from evil and alerts us to the dangers of self-centeredness, godly fear also attracts us to majesty of God, fostering awe, attachment, and allegiance that are constitutive of faith. To fear God is to bow before his might and take refuge in his power. To be drawn to God in fear is to be still and know that he is sovereign God, a truth which can calm inner fears and anxious hearts. To revere God's glory is to stand in amazement at what he has done and surrender ourselves to his service. To be terrified at his wrath is to come and receive his grace to be rescued from the path of condemnation. To treat the holy God as the one and only and in a category by himself is also to love and adore him as our God. Different than all the other fears that plague us, a healthy and holy fear of God draws us in to relationship with God.

<Callout>Godly parenting in the fear of the Lord teaches children to relish the Lord as much as revere him.</Callout> Not accidentally does this psalm put the fear of the Lord alongside a personal experience of God's goodness. Faithful parenting aims to help children "taste and see that the Lord is good" (34:9). Parental work seeks to help children to unquestionably know for themselves (through experience of personal faith) that God is good. In fact, parental work as a whole is training a child's appetites for what is truly God. A faith-building curriculum in the fear of God is a appetite-forming endeavor to train desires for what is truly good. to When parents teach their children the fear of God, they introduce them to a fear that fosters heartfelt worship, strengthens childlike faith and dependence, shapes desires, results in divine blessing, and triumphs over all other fears. A faith defined by such holy fear cannot but result in life transformation and spiritual growth.

###### The Man Who Delights in Life: Aiming for Deep Transformation in Character Formation

Biblical fear of God produces godly character and conduct. According to the psalm, those who fear the Lord "keep [their] tongue from evil" (34:13), "turn away from evil" (34:14), "do good" (34:14), and "seek peace and pursue it" (34:13). Those who embody godliness in life are those who have tasted the goodness of the Lord (34:8). Therefore, the person satisfied in the Lord practices righteous living as a person who "delights in life" (34:12). Those who reject evil and do good are those who have a desire for "good days" (34:12). Faithful followers are called "righteous" (34:15) in contrast with the wicked who "do evil" (34:16). In fact, those who fear the Lord experience God's divine favor: "the eyes of the Lord are on the righteous, and His ears are inclined to their cry" (34:15). <Callout>Genuine faith in God that is expressed in a holy fear of God results in deep, dynamic, and enduring change in life and character.</Callout>

> Parenting work is a matter of moral formation. This does not mean that parental work is reduced to mere behavioral modification. While godly parenting

upholds God's commands as the code of their household, moral formation is more than mere compliance to ethical commands. Yet focusing on good behavior, well-mannered politeness, and social courteousness is where most parents remain. Although parents should attend to a child's behavior—external conduct—the parental work aims at a much deeper change—godly character. When speaking of God's people, this psalm characterizes them as godly in speech, action, character, desire, and purpose. As a summary, those who fear the Lord in genuine faith are called righteous—mirroring the character of God. The character of their lives is fundamentally oriented to God that it has taken on a God-shaped form. Christian homebuilding is a matter of faith formation toward a transformation in godliness—a change at the level of one's person, being, and very self. Children who modify their behavior only to placate parental nagging usually do not sustain their adjusted conduct when the pressures of the world mounts. Instead, they quickly conform to the patterns of the world. But those who have a fundamental change in character—due to a deep and abiding faith in and fear of God—maintain their godliness in the face of temptation, sin, injustice, cultural pressure, and targeted suffering.

Parenting toward character formation requires parents themselves to embody the character they hope to inculcate in their children. By far, the most powerful tool in teaching is imitation. Students learn best by modeling themselves after their teachers. This is true for the development of skills and it is especially true in the area of virtue formation. No better place than the home is there for children to grow in character. In their parents, there should be a model of godliness worthy of emulation. This is where parenting becomes one of God's greatest instruments of sanctification. Our children are often mirrors of our own selves—reflecting back to us our gifts and our flaws. Once we recognize the importance of character formation and the power of our individual example, our children also become a motivation for us to take seriously our own formation in godliness. Our children will watch our speech, conduct, time management, relationships, and attitudes and often take after us. They will observe how we endure difficulty and learn to face hardship in ways we have modeled to them. Rather than shirking this duty as a teacher, let us embrace the fact that God has intertwined our own personal faith formation with the character development of our children.

###### The Afflictions of the Righteous: Raising Children Who Can Be Righteous Sufferers

Christian parents are called to train their children to maintain faith in God in a godless and hostile world. While the wicked face judgment, receive punishment, and experience just condemnation (34:21), the righteous will be delivered (34:22). The promise of ultimate deliverance for the righteous does not mean that they will not presently suffer. In fact, the ending of this psalm is a faithful testimony of the "afflictions of the righteous" (34:19). Biblical fear of the Lord draws God's faithful followers to deeper and more intimate trust in the Lord, knowing that they will encounter difficulty as they follow the Lord. Those who fear God rightly will cling to him desperately. When difficulties rise "the righteous cry out" (34:17) to the Lord for help. God's people oppressed by "all their troubles" (34:17) look to the Lord for deliverance. The "brokenhearted" (34:18) seek God's comfort and the "contrite in spirit" (34:18) plead for God's rescue. In the end, the fear of the Lord causes the righteous to "take refuge in Him" (34:22). The righteous sufferer knows that God hears his cry (34:17), provides complete deliverance (34:17, 19), offers his comforting presnce (34:18), secures salvation (34:18), protects his own (34:20) and redeems his devoted servants (34:22).

Popular and cultural parenting seeks to mitigate suffering in the life of their children. While Christian parents do bear a responsibility to protect and guard their families, godly households are places that prepare children to suffer well and maintain faith in God. During the work of Christian homebuilding, parents must seriously warn about the plight of the wicked, summoning children to fear of the Lord expressed in genuine faith. Genuine faith warns children about the path of destruction if they nurse a casual, dismissive, or irreverent attitude to God. But parents must also give an account of the righteous suffering—an honest portrait that God's people will endure hardship. We must not shirk from this duty. <Callout>The fear of the Lord establishes a hearty and unfeigned faith in God that outfits our growing children to withstand unspeakable pain and difficulty.</Callout> <Callout>What a wonderful and demanding parental vocation: calling children Godward in faith and sending them into the world to suffer as righteous followers of Jesus who take refuge in God.</Callout>

###### Conclusion

In this psalm, we learn about an important goal of parenting: character development in the fear of God. The logic of the passage is timeless and speaks to all generations and all cultures. Christian homebuilding fosters a family life of continual worship, establishing habits of worship that teach our children to continually live in the presence of God. Our most basic lesson must be to summon our children to fear God—taking his righteousness seriously, honor his glorious worthy, and relishing his unalloyed goodness. Healthy fear of God is an expression of genuine faith that results in deep character transformation. In turn, godly character represents such a dynamic change in a person's life that they can be a righteous sufferer in a hostile world. In sum, <Callout>godly parents aim for children to experience God for themselves in all his glory, his goodness, his righteousness, and his gracious deliverance.</Callout> May God help us to build hearth and home with our spiritual mantle bearing witness to the fear the Lord!

##### Session Summary

- The Christian home should be a place of continual worship.
- Christians parents are the primary teachers of their children. The ultimate aim of their instruction should be to teach their children the fear of God.
- The fear of God produces lasting change of godly character. Parents need to orient their parenting work towards character formation.
- Godly parents prepare children to be righteous sufferers. Parenting work should help prepare children to face hardship, suffering, and persecution without abandoning their faith in God.

#### Charting the Path Ahead

*Reflect on the session and envision next steps. Record key takeaways from the lesson. Conclude your time by praying as small groups and a whole community. Review next steps.*

##### Small Group Prayer

<Question id="Home-ParOnePre-SesFou-Ses4ComStu-ChaPatAhe-SmaGroPra-1">How can I pray for **other members** of my community?</Question>

##### Corporate Prayer

Heavenly Lord, we have tasted and we have seen that you are good. We have experienced the blessing of taking refuge in the Lord. We have come to enjoy the freedom of the fear of the Lord. There cannot be an end to your praise because there is no end to your wonderful works. Your praise must be continually on our lips because there is no match for the perfection of your beauty, the splendor of your character, or the glory of your majesty. We want our parenting to be an act of worship. Help us to instruct our children in the fear of God, first embracing this fear in our own lives. Equip us to raise our children in godliness and holiness, that their core character would be conformed to Christ. Form in our children the faith of a righteous sufferer, one who clings to you in the best and the worst of times. We are unfit for this task. Open your eyes to us, and see our need. Incline your ears to us, and hear our cries. Turn your face toward us, and show us your mercy. Stretch our your arm, and help us in our weakness. Help us, O Lord, we prayer. Amen.

- Meet with a mentor to review this session.
- Memorize the biblical proverb.
- Complete the spiritual disciplines over the course of the week.
- Finish your personal and parenting project.

Fix these words of mine in your hearts and minds; tie them as reminders on your hands and bind them on your foreheads. Teach them to your children, speaking about them when you sit at home and when you walk along the road, when you lie down and when you get up. Write them on the doorposts of your houses and on your gates, so that as long as the heavens are above the earth, your days and those of your children may be multiplied in the land that the Lord swore to give your fathers.

Assemble the people—men, women, children, and the foreigners within your gates so that they may listen and learn to fear the Lord your God and to follow carefully all the words of this law. 13 Then their children who do not know the law will listen and learn to fear the Lord your God, as long as you live in the land that you are crossing the Jordan to possess.

Listen, my son, to your father's instruction, and do not forsake the teaching of your mother. For they are a garland of grace on your head and a pendant around your neck.

My son, do not forget my teaching, but let your heart keep my commandments; for they will add length to your days, years and peace to your life. Never let loving devotion a or faithfulness leave you; bind them around your neck, write them on the tablet of your heart. Then you will find favor and high regard in the sight of God and man

Listen, my sons, to a father's instruction; pay attention and gain understanding. 2 For I give you sound teaching; do not abandon my directive. 3 When I was a son to my father, tender and the only child of my mother, 4 he taught me and said, "Let your heart lay hold of my words; keep my commands and you will live. 5 Get wisdom, get understanding; do not forget my words or turn from them.

"Honor your father and mother" (which is the first commandment with a promise), "that it may go well with you and that you may have a long life on the earth." Fathers, do not provoke your children to wrath; instead, bring them up in the discipline and instruction of the Lord.

> Make our home a place that fears the Lord. We know that we cannot cultivate this home without embodying it ourselves. We gaze at your glory, and recognize there is none like you. We come to terms with your justice and kneel before you in humble repentance. We acknowledge your majesty, and fall in awe of your beauty. We are confronted by your righteousness and confess you as the Most High God. We taste your goodness and count you as our ultimate good. Help us to live in the fear of the Lord. By fearing you, may we triumph over all our fears. Amen

##### Project Instructions

Reflect on the sacred call of parenting as a vocation in faith formation. As you recall the biblical passage studied in this session, contemplate the responsibilities involved in this work. As you write your resolutions, use incorporate the principles/teachings of the passage in your statements of commitment.

##### Example Resolutions

- I resolve to guide my children in a life of humble obedience to God's commandments.
- I resolve to teach my children the true character of God and that there is only one God to whom deserves our full allegiance and worship.
- I resolve to encourage my children to love God with their whole being.
- I resolve to care for the whole person of each of my children: body, mind, heart, and soul.
- I resolve to establish God-honoring traditions in my family in order to build a faith memory in my children and in my household so that the faith will transfer intact to the next generations.
